Distinguishing Khabar from Talab: The Two Fundamental Categories of Speech
Author classifies speech into khabar and talab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given Arabic utterance is khabar or talab based on its truth-aptness.

Core Idea
Only statements that can be true or false belong to logic; requests are outside its scope.

This passage divides all used speech into two categories: khabar (informative) and talab (request). This division is foundational for logic because logic only deals with propositions that admit truth and falsehood. In this lesson, we will learn how to distinguish between these two types and understand why the logician limits his investigation to khabar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that the text divides used words into two: khabar and talab.
2
Check for truth-aptness
For any given utterance, ask: Can it be true or false? If yes, it's khabar. If it conveys a request, it's talab.
3
Note the logician's focus
Understand that the purpose of this distinction is to delimit the subject matter of logic to only khabar, not talab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخبر والإنشاء. اللفظ المستعمل إن أفاد الطلب كـ (اضرب) و (لا تقم)، فهو يسمى طلباً، وإن احتمل الصدق والكذب، نحو زيد قائم، فهو يسمى خبراً. والطلب يقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام: فإن كان مع استعلاء وهو إظهار الطالب العلوّ على المطلوب منه فهو أمر، وإن كان الطلب لا مع استعلاء بل مع خضوع وإظهار الطالب الانخفاض عن المطلوب منه فإنه يسمى دعاء، وفي حال إظهار الطالب المساواة للمطلوب منه فهو التماس. والغرض من التقسيم بيان الخبر، لأن المنطقي لا يبحث إلا عن الخبر، ولا بحث له عن الطلب بأقسامه. ثم في تفسير الاستعلاء: أى وإن كان الطالب أعلى من المطلوب منه كالسيد من عبده من حيث هو سيده والملك من وزيره من حيث هو كذلك. وفي تفسير الخضوع: أى وإن كان الطالب أدنى منه كالعبد لسيّده من حيث هو عبده، ووزير الملك له من حيث هو وزيره والجاهل للعالم من حيث هو جاهل.
Khabar and Insha': The used word if it conveys a request, like 'strike!' and 'do not stand!', then it is called talab (request). If it admits truth and falsehood, like 'Zaid is standing', then it is called khabar (statement). And talab is divided into three sections: if it is with superiority — that is, the requester showing supremacy over the one requested — then it is amr (command). If the request is not with superiority but with humility and the requester showing lowliness relative to the one requested, then it is called du'a (supplication). And in the case of the requester showing equality with the one requested, then it is iltimas (entreaty). The purpose of the division is to clarify khabar, because the logician only investigates khabar, and he has no investigation of talab and its divisions. Then in explanation of superiority: that is, if the requester is higher than the one requested, like the master to his slave in terms of being his master, and the king to his minister in terms of that. And in explanation of humility: that is, if the requester is lower than him, like the slave to his master in terms of being his slave, and the minister to the king in terms of being his minister, and the ignorant to the scholar in terms of being ignorant.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage classifies speech (lafz musta'mal) into two main types: khabar (informative) which can be true or false, and talab (request) which cannot. The author then further divides talab into three kinds based on the relative status of the requester and the one requested: (1) amr (command) when the requester is superior, (2) du'a (supplication) when the requester is inferior or shows humility, and (3) iltmas (entreaty) when there is equality. The footnote adds examples: for du'a, like a slave asking his master, or a minister to his king; for amr, like a master commanding his slave. The passage emphasizes that logic only concerns itself with khabar, because only khabar can be evaluated for truth, which is the proper subject of mantiq.

Key Terms
الخبر
الطلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a logician not study talab?
A) Because talab is not Arabic
B) Because talab cannot be true or false
C) Because talab is only used in poetry
D) Because talab is not from Allah

Key Takeaway
The first step in logical analysis is recognizing whether an utterance is a truth-apt proposition (khabar) or a non-truth-apt request (talab).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following Arabic utterance as khabar or talab: 'الله أكبر' (Allahu Akbar). Is it a statement that can be true or false, or a request? (Answer: It is khabar, because it affirms a fact that can be true/false.)
?
Give an example of a talab from the Qur'an that is a du'a (supplication) and explain why it is not studied by logicians.

The Three Kinds of Talab: Amr, Du'a, and Iltimas
Author divides talab into amr, du'a, and iltmas based on relative status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between amr, du'a, and iltmas based on the relative status of requester and requested.

Core Idea
A request changes name depending on whether the speaker is superior, inferior, or equal to the hearer.

After defining talab as a non-truth-apt request, the author divides it into three types based on the hierarchical relationship between the requester and the one requested. Understanding these distinctions is important for interpreting commands in Islamic law and for recognizing that logic sets aside all requests, regardless of type. This lesson will help you identify each type and understand its definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of division
The basis is the relative status: superiority (istila'), humility (khudu'), and equality (musawa).
2
Recognize amr
When the requester is superior, it is amr (command). Example: a master to his slave.
The passage says: 'with superiority'
3
Recognize du'a
When the requester is inferior or shows humility, it is du'a (supplication). Example: a slave to his master.
The passage says: 'with humility'
4
Recognize iltmas
When the requester and requested are equals, it is iltmas (entreaty).
The passage says: 'showing equality'
5
Confirm the logician's stance
All three are talab, and thus not studied by logicians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخبر والإنشاء. اللفظ المستعمل إن أفاد الطلب كـ (اضرب) و (لا تقم)، فهو يسمى طلباً، وإن احتمل الصدق والكذب، نحو زيد قائم، فهو يسمى خبراً. والطلب يقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام: فإن كان مع استعلاء وهو إظهار الطالب العلوّ على المطلوب منه فهو أمر، وإن كان الطلب لا مع استعلاء بل مع خضوع وإظهار الطالب الانخفاض عن المطلوب منه فإنه يسمى دعاء، وفي حال إظهار الطالب المساواة للمطلوب منه فهو التماس. والغرض من التقسيم بيان الخبر، لأن المنطقي لا يبحث إلا عن الخبر، ولا بحث له عن الطلب بأقسامه. ثم في تفسير الاستعلاء: أى وإن كان الطالب أعلى من المطلوب منه كالسيد من عبده من حيث هو سيده والملك من وزيره من حيث هو كذلك. وفي تفسير الخضوع: أى وإن كان الطالب أدنى منه كالعبد لسيّده من حيث هو عبده، ووزير الملك له من حيث هو وزيره والجاهل للعالم من حيث هو جاهل.
Khabar and Insha': The used word if it conveys a request, like 'strike!' and 'do not stand!', then it is called talab (request). If it admits truth and falsehood, like 'Zaid is standing', then it is called khabar (statement). And talab is divided into three sections: if it is with superiority — that is, the requester showing supremacy over the one requested — then it is amr (command). If the request is not with superiority but with humility and the requester showing lowliness relative to the one requested, then it is called du'a (supplication). And in the case of the requester showing equality with the one requested, then it is iltimas (entreaty). The purpose of the division is to clarify khabar, because the logician only investigates khabar, and he has no investigation of talab and its divisions. Then in explanation of superiority: that is, if the requester is higher than the one requested, like the master to his slave in terms of being his master, and the king to his minister in terms of that. And in explanation of humility: that is, if the requester is lower than him, like the slave to his master in terms of being his slave, and the minister to the king in terms of being his minister, and the ignorant to the scholar in terms of being ignorant.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage classifies speech (lafz musta'mal) into two main types: khabar (informative) which can be true or false, and talab (request) which cannot. The author then further divides talab into three kinds based on the relative status of the requester and the one requested: (1) amr (command) when the requester is superior, (2) du'a (supplication) when the requester is inferior or shows humility, and (3) iltmas (entreaty) when there is equality. The footnote adds examples: for du'a, like a slave asking his master, or a minister to his king; for amr, like a master commanding his slave. The passage emphasizes that logic only concerns itself with khabar, because only khabar can be evaluated for truth, which is the proper subject of mantiq.

Key Terms
الأمر
الدعاء
الالتماس
الاستعلاء
الخضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the distinguishing feature of du'a?
A) It is a request from a superior
B) It is a request with humility
C) It is a request between equals
D) It is not a request at all

Key Takeaway
The three kinds of talab are defined by the hierarchical relationship between speaker and hearer; all are excluded from logical investigation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following Qur'anic request: 'رَبَّنَا اغْفِرْ لَنَا' (Our Lord, forgive us). Is it amr, du'a, or iltmas? (Answer: du'a, because the requester is inferior.)

Universal Negation vs. Negated Conjunction in the Hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn
Analyzes the logical form of the Prophet's negation in the Dhu al-Yadayn hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a universal negative proposition ('none of them') and a negation of conjunction ('not both'), and apply this to analyze the Prophet's response in the hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn.

Core Idea
Negating each individually is not the same as negating their conjunction—a key logical distinction for correct inference.

In the hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn, the Prophet was asked whether he had shortened the prayer or forgotten. His reply 'none of that occurred' is interpreted by some as negating only the combination, but the commentator argues it is a universal negation of each individually. This lesson unpacks the logical structure behind that interpretation, using the rules of contradictory propositions and the scope of 'kull' (all).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Original Question and Response
Recall the hadith: Dhu al-Yadayn asked the Prophet, 'Have you shortened the prayer or forgotten?' The Prophet replied, 'None of that occurred.' Note the question structure: it presents two possibilities (shortening or forgetfulness) with 'am' (or), asking for specification.
The questioner did not believe both happened, only one.
2
Parse the Two Possible Logical Forms
Interpretation A: The negation applies to the conjunction – 'not (shortening and forgetfulness together)'. Interpretation B: The negation applies to each individually – 'no shortening and no forgetfulness'. Write both as logical propositions.
Interpretation B is a universal negative (¬S ∧ ¬F), while interpretation A is a negated conjunction (¬(S ∧ F)).
3
Apply the Rule of Contradictories
The commentator notes that a particular affirmative (e.g., 'some of that occurred') only contradicts a universal negative, not a negated conjunction. Since Dhu al-Yadayn said 'some of that occurred', the only statement that directly contradicts it is the universal negative (none of them). This supports interpretation B.
Recall: Particular affirmative (I) and universal negative (E) are contradictories.
4
Analyze the Scope of 'Kull' (All)
The commentator states that when 'kull' precedes the negation, it yields 'umum al-salab' (generality of negation) – the negation applies to each individual separately. In the hadith, the phrase 'all of that' (kull dhalik) has 'kull' before the negation, so the negation is universal.
Conversely, if negation preceded 'kull', it would be 'salb al-‘umum' (negation of generality).
5
Evaluate the Commentator's Critique
The commentator concludes that the author's example (using this hadith as an instance of negated conjunction) is incorrect. Understand why: the stronger interpretation is universal negation, so the example fails to illustrate the intended logical point.
The commentator also mentions that such weak examples are sometimes included deliberately to provoke student inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجتمعين، لا نفي كل على حدته، وهذا تأويل مرجوح ، والراجح أن المقصود نفي كلّ من القصر والنسيان على حدته، فيكون سلبا كلياً ، لأن السؤال بأم عن أحد الأمرين لطلب التعيين، فجوابه إما بالتعيين وإما بنفي كل منها، لا بنفي اجتماعهما، لأن السائل لم يعتقد الاجتماع، وإنما اعتقد ثبوت واحد منهما. ولأنه قد روي أن ذا اليدين قال له: (بل بعض ذلك قد كان، وهذا إنما يناقض نفي كل منهما لا نفي اجتماعها، لما تقرّر من أن الموجبة الجزئية إنما تناقض السالبة الكلية ، ولأن القاعدة الغالبة أن (كل) إذا تقدمت على النفي كان الكلام من عموم السلب، وكل متقدمة هنا في كل ذلك لم يكن فيكون السلب عاماً لكل فرد بحسب الظن لا بحسب الواقع، فلا كذب، وحينئذ فتمثيل الناظم بهذا المثل غير صحيح. (5) قوله (بحسب الظن أى لأن الجواب إنما يكون بحسب الاعتقاد وغلبة الظن. وقوله (المثل غيــر صـحيح) لا يقدح في الناظم إذ البحث في المثال ليس من دأب الفحول ويلوح لى والله أعلم أن العلماء يقصدون وضع المسامحات في كتب المبتدئين، وبعض الوجوه الضعيفة لحث الطالب على الاعتراض، فيكثر البحث وتزداد الفائدة، وإلا فإنك تجدهم في الحواشي والمطولات في غاية التدقيق لا تفوتهم مثل هذه الأمور. فلا يُظن أن العلماء وبخاصة المحققين منهم تخفى عليه أخطاء واضحة يمكن للمبتدى أن يكشفها. والله أعلم.
Together, not the negation of each individually. And this is a weak interpretation. The stronger one is that the intended meaning is the negation of each of shortening and forgetfulness individually, so it is a universal negation. Because the question with 'am' (or) asks for specification between two matters, so its answer is either specification or negation of each, not negation of their conjunction, because the questioner did not believe in the conjunction, but rather believed in the affirmation of one of them. And because it has been narrated that Dhu al-Yadayn said to him: 'Rather some of that occurred.' And this only contradicts the negation of each of them, not the negation of their conjunction, as established that the particular affirmative only contradicts the universal negative. And because the predominant rule is that when 'kull' (all) precedes the negation, the expression is from the generality of negation, and 'kull' is preceding here in 'all of that' so the negation is general for every individual according to assumption, not according to reality, so there is no lie. Then the author's exemplification with this example is incorrect. (5) His statement 'according to assumption' means the answer is only according to belief and preponderance of assumption. And his statement 'the example is incorrect' does not disparage the author, since research into the example is not the practice of great scholars. It appears to me—and Allah knows best—that scholars intend to place leniencies in books for beginners, and some weak aspects to encourage the student to object, so that research multiplies and benefit increases. Otherwise, you find them in glosses and lengthy works in extreme precision, not missing such matters. So it should not be thought that scholars, especially the investigators among them, are unaware of clear errors that a beginner can uncover. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of a logical example used by the author of the matn. The commentator argues that the proper interpretation of the Prophet's response to Dhu al-Yadayn is that he negated both forgetfulness and shortening individually (universal negation), not that he negated their conjunction. This is supported by the rule that a particular affirmative (like 'some of that occurred') only contradicts a universal negative. The commentator also provides a defense of the author, suggesting that such weak examples are intentionally included to provoke student inquiry. All examples used are from the hadith and Islamic creed, such as 'Every soul will taste death' and 'There is no god but Allah.'

Key Terms
قضية موجبة جزئية (qadiyya mūjiba juz’iyya): A particular affirmative proposition, e.g., 'some of that occurred'. [Note: Stable term across logical texts.]
سالبة كلية (sāliba kulliyya): A universal negative proposition, e.g., 'none of that occurred'. [Note: Stable term.]
عموم السلب (‘umūm al-salab): Generality of negation, applying to each individual separately. [Note: Contrasted with 'salb al-‘umūm' (negation of generality) in later works.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does the Prophet's negation in the hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn constitute a universal negative rather than a negated conjunction?
A) Because the particular affirmative only contradicts a universal negative.
B) Because the questioner asked about both possibilities together.
C) Because the Prophet used the word 'all' before the negation.
D) Because the hadith is about forgetfulness and shortening.

Key Takeaway
When 'kull' precedes a negation, it produces a universal negative that negates each individual, not just their conjunction—a crucial distinction for accurate logical analysis of Islamic texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following statement is a universal negation or a negated conjunction: 'The Prophet did not shorten nor forget' (from the hadith). Justify your answer using the rules discussed.
?

What is a Proposition? The Basic Unit of Assent
Author defines the proposition as the unit of assent and truth-assessment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a proposition (qadiyya) and distinguish it from a concept (tasawwur).

Core Idea
A proposition is the unit of truth and falsehood — the building block of assent.

After mastering concepts, logic turns to propositions — statements that can be true or false. This lesson anchors the definition and the shift from concepts to assents.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition in the matn
Find the line: 'ما احتمل الصدق لذاته جرى بينهم قضية وخبرا.'
2
Parse the definition
The definition is 'that which admits truth for its own sake.' Note that a proposition must have the capacity for truth/falsehood intrinsically, not by external consideration.
3
Connect the definition to the broader aim of logic
The passage tells us assents (tasdiqat) are the main objective; propositions are the principles of assents.
4
Distinguish a proposition from a concept
A concept (like 'man') is neither true nor false; a proposition (like 'Zayd is a man') is truth-apt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابٌ في القَضَايَا وَأَحْكامِها . بعد الانتهاء من البحث في التصورات، مبادئها ومقاصدها ، شرع في البحث في التصديقات، وهي المطلوب الأهم في علم المنطق. وللتصديقات قسمان: مبادئ ومقاصد، فالمبادئ هي البحث في القضايا وأحكامها، والمقاصد هي البحث في الأقيسة. ونبدأ الآن في البحث في المبادئ. قال: ما احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ لِذاتِهِ جَرى بَيْنَهُمُ قَضِيَّةً وَخَبَـرا . ثمَّ القَضَايَا عِنْدَهُم قِسْمانِ شَرْطِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيةٌ وَالتّان . وَالثَّاني كُلِّيَّةٌ شَخْصِيَّةٌ وَالأَوَّلُ إِمَّا مُسَوَّرٌ وَإِمَّا مُهْمَلُ . وَالسُّورُ كُلِّيَّاً وَجُزئياً يُرَى وَأَرْبَعٌ أَقْسَامُهُ حَيْثُ جَرى . إِمَّا بِكُلِّ أَوْ بِبَعْضٍ أَوْ بلا شَيْءَ وَلَيْسَ بَعْضُ أَوْ شَبَهِ جَلا . (1) أى من أقسام كتب المنطق، أي أبوابها. وقد مرّ أنّ للتصوّر قسمين. فأبواب المنطـق أربعـة. وأمـا بحـث الدلالات ومباحث الألفاظ إنما ذكرت في كتب المنطق لتوقف بحث الكليات الخمس عليهـا. ومـن اعتبـر أقسام القياس الخمسة التي تعرف بالصناعات الخمس، عد الأبواب ثمانية. أى عد للتصورات مبـادى ومقاصد، وعد للتصديقات مبادى وخمسة مقاصد. ومنهم من جعل مباحث الألفاظ مما يحتاج إليه باباً مستقلاً فجعـل أبوابه تسعة.
Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings. After completing the research on conceptualizations (taṣawwurāt), their principles and aims, he began research on assents (taṣdīqāt), which is the most important demand in the science of logic. And for assents there are two parts: principles and aims. The principles are the research on propositions and their rulings, and the aims are the research on syllogisms. And we now begin research on the principles. He said: That which admits truth for its own sake is called among them a proposition and a report. Then propositions with them are two divisions: conditional and categorical, and the two. And the second (i.e., categorical) is universal and individual; and the first (i.e., conditional) is either quantified or neglected. And the quantifier is seen as universal and particular, and its divisions are four when it occurs: either by 'every' or 'some' or 'no' (nothing) and 'not some' or its clear equivalent. (1) Meaning from the divisions of books of logic, i.e., its chapters. It has passed that for conceptualization there are two divisions. So the chapters of logic are four. As for the study of significations (dalālāt) and topics of words, they are only mentioned in books of logic because the research on the five universals depends on them. And whoever considers the five divisions of syllogism known as the five arts, counts the chapters as eight. That is, he counts for conceptualizations: principles and aims, and for assents: principles and five aims. And some of them made the topics of words as an independent chapter needed, so they made its chapters nine.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on propositions (qadāyā). It first situates this chapter within the broader structure of logic: after finishing the study of concepts (taṣawwurāt), the author moves to assents (taṣdīqāt), which are the main objective of logic. Assents are divided into principles (propositions) and aims (syllogisms). The matn (verse) then defines a proposition as 'that which admits truth for its own sake', also called a report (khabar). It divides propositions into two main types: conditional (sharṭiyya) and categorical (ḥamliyya). The categorical proposition is further divided into universal (kulliyya) and individual (shakhṣiyya); the conditional is either quantified (musawwar) or neglected (muhmal). The quantifier (sūr) is universal or particular, and there are four types: 'all' (kull), 'some' (ba‘ḍ), 'no' (lā shay‘), and 'not some' (laysa ba‘ḍ) or its equivalent. The footnote explains the structural division of logic books: usually four chapters (concepts, propositions, syllogisms, and the five arts), but some add an independent chapter on words, making nine.

Key Terms
قضية
خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining feature of a proposition?
A) It is composed of subject and predicate.
B) It admits truth for its own sake.
C) It is always conditional.
D) It is a concept.

Key Takeaway
A proposition is any utterance that can be true or false in itself, forming the basis of assent in logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Two Main Types: Categorical and Conditional
Author divides propositions into categorical and conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a categorical proposition from a conditional proposition and give an example of each from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Every proposition is either a simple predication (categorical) or a compound with condition (conditional).

The matn divides propositions into two primary types: categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). Understanding this division is key to classifying statements used in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Find the division in the matn
Look at line: 'ثم القضايا عندهم قسمان شرطية حملية والتان.'
2
Define categorical (hamliyya)
A proposition that predicates one thing of another, e.g., 'Zayd is standing'.
3
Define conditional (shartiyya)
A proposition composed of two parts (antecedent and consequent) connected by a condition, e.g., 'If the sun rises, then it is day'.
4
Classify examples from Islamic sciences
Categorical: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' Conditional: 'If a woman menstruates, then she does not pray.'
5
Note that the division covers all propositions
No other type exists; every proposition is either categorical or conditional.

بَابٌ في القَضَايَا وَأَحْكامِها . بعد الانتهاء من البحث في التصورات، مبادئها ومقاصدها ، شرع في البحث في التصديقات، وهي المطلوب الأهم في علم المنطق. وللتصديقات قسمان: مبادئ ومقاصد، فالمبادئ هي البحث في القضايا وأحكامها، والمقاصد هي البحث في الأقيسة. ونبدأ الآن في البحث في المبادئ. قال: ما احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ لِذاتِهِ جَرى بَيْنَهُمُ قَضِيَّةً وَخَبَـرا . ثمَّ القَضَايَا عِنْدَهُم قِسْمانِ شَرْطِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيةٌ وَالتّان . وَالثَّاني كُلِّيَّةٌ شَخْصِيَّةٌ وَالأَوَّلُ إِمَّا مُسَوَّرٌ وَإِمَّا مُهْمَلُ . وَالسُّورُ كُلِّيَّاً وَجُزئياً يُرَى وَأَرْبَعٌ أَقْسَامُهُ حَيْثُ جَرى . إِمَّا بِكُلِّ أَوْ بِبَعْضٍ أَوْ بلا شَيْءَ وَلَيْسَ بَعْضُ أَوْ شَبَهِ جَلا . (1) أى من أقسام كتب المنطق، أي أبوابها. وقد مرّ أنّ للتصوّر قسمين. فأبواب المنطـق أربعـة. وأمـا بحـث الدلالات ومباحث الألفاظ إنما ذكرت في كتب المنطق لتوقف بحث الكليات الخمس عليهـا. ومـن اعتبـر أقسام القياس الخمسة التي تعرف بالصناعات الخمس، عد الأبواب ثمانية. أى عد للتصورات مبـادى ومقاصد، وعد للتصديقات مبادى وخمسة مقاصد. ومنهم من جعل مباحث الألفاظ مما يحتاج إليه باباً مستقلاً فجعـل أبوابه تسعة.
Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings. After completing the research on conceptualizations (taṣawwurāt), their principles and aims, he began research on assents (taṣdīqāt), which is the most important demand in the science of logic. And for assents there are two parts: principles and aims. The principles are the research on propositions and their rulings, and the aims are the research on syllogisms. And we now begin research on the principles. He said: That which admits truth for its own sake is called among them a proposition and a report. Then propositions with them are two divisions: conditional and categorical, and the two. And the second (i.e., categorical) is universal and individual; and the first (i.e., conditional) is either quantified or neglected. And the quantifier is seen as universal and particular, and its divisions are four when it occurs: either by 'every' or 'some' or 'no' (nothing) and 'not some' or its clear equivalent. (1) Meaning from the divisions of books of logic, i.e., its chapters. It has passed that for conceptualization there are two divisions. So the chapters of logic are four. As for the study of significations (dalālāt) and topics of words, they are only mentioned in books of logic because the research on the five universals depends on them. And whoever considers the five divisions of syllogism known as the five arts, counts the chapters as eight. That is, he counts for conceptualizations: principles and aims, and for assents: principles and five aims. And some of them made the topics of words as an independent chapter needed, so they made its chapters nine.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on propositions (qadāyā). It first situates this chapter within the broader structure of logic: after finishing the study of concepts (taṣawwurāt), the author moves to assents (taṣdīqāt), which are the main objective of logic. Assents are divided into principles (propositions) and aims (syllogisms). The matn (verse) then defines a proposition as 'that which admits truth for its own sake', also called a report (khabar). It divides propositions into two main types: conditional (sharṭiyya) and categorical (ḥamliyya). The categorical proposition is further divided into universal (kulliyya) and individual (shakhṣiyya); the conditional is either quantified (musawwar) or neglected (muhmal). The quantifier (sūr) is universal or particular, and there are four types: 'all' (kull), 'some' (ba‘ḍ), 'no' (lā shay‘), and 'not some' (laysa ba‘ḍ) or its equivalent. The footnote explains the structural division of logic books: usually four chapters (concepts, propositions, syllogisms, and the five arts), but some add an independent chapter on words, making nine.

Quantifiers: Universal, Particular, and the Four Forms
Author details quantifiers and their four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantifier (sūr) in a proposition and classify it as one of the four types: 'every', 'some', 'no', 'not some'.

Core Idea
Quantifiers determine the scope of a proposition: all, some, none, or not some.

The passage further classifies categorical propositions by quantity (universal/individual) and conditional by quantification (musawwar/muhmal). It then lists the four quantifier particles. This lesson focuses on the quantifiers used in logic.

Guided Path
1
Note the two subdivisions
The matn divides categorical into universal (kulliyya) and individual (shakhsiyya); conditional into quantified (musawwar) and neglected (muhmal).
2
Focus on the quantifiers (sūr)
The matn says quantifier is universal or particular, and its divisions are four.
3
Memorize the four quantifier words
(1) كل (kull - every), (2) بعض (ba'd - some), (3) لا شيء (la shay' - no), (4) ليس بعض (laysa ba'd - not some) or its equivalent.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take a proposition like 'Every Muslim must pray.' Quantifier: every (kull) – universal affirmative.

What Makes a Sentence a Logical Proposition?
Author defines proposition and its three conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether any given sentence is a proposition (qadiyya) by applying the three essential conditions: complete expression, truth/falsity evaluability, and inherentness.

Core Idea
A proposition is not just any sentence; it must inherently admit truth and falsity.

In this passage, the author defines the foundational unit of logic: the proposition (qadiyya). Understanding what qualifies as a proposition is critical because all logical reasoning operates on propositions. The author provides three conditions that must be met. We will break down each condition using examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions from the definition
From the definition 'the expression that admits truth and falsity inherently', extract three conditions: (1) it is a complete composite expression, (2) it admits truth and falsity, and (3) this admission is inherent (not accidental).
The word 'inherently' (lidhatihi) is crucial.
2
Distinguish complete composite from incomplete composites
Not all expressions are complete composites. Excluded are restrictive compositions like 'the standing Zayd' or 'the book of the teacher', which lack a complete statement. Only full sentences that can stand alone as a claim are candidates.
Complete composite here means a sentence that can be evaluated as true or false.
3
Recognize non-assertoric sentences that cannot be true or false
Sentences like commands ('Pray!'), prohibitions ('Do not steal!'), questions ('What time is it?'), and performatives ('I sold you this house.') do not admit truth or falsity. They are not propositions because one cannot truthfully call them true or false.
This excludes all insha' (non-assertoric) sentences.
4
Understand 'inherently' vs accidental truth
A sentence may be true by accident (e.g., 'Zayd is standing' could be true at one moment but false later). However, the condition 'inherently' means the sentence itself, by its very structure, is capable of being true or false. The truth value may depend on circumstances, but the capacity is inherent.
This excludes sentences whose truth is derivative or dependent on other propositions.
5
Apply to examples from Islamic sciences
Take sentences from Fiqh: 'Wine is forbidden' – is it a proposition? Yes, because it is a complete statement admitting truth/falsity inherently. 'Do not drink wine' – not a proposition, it is a command. Practice classifying more examples.
The key is checking all three conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ لِذاتِهِ جَرى بَيْنَهُمُ قَضِيَّةً وَخَبَـرا
أقول: القضية مشتقة من القضاء وهو الحكم، لاشتمالها عليه. والمقصود من أحكام القضايا التناقض والعكس. وأما تعريف القضية فهي اللفظ (1) الذي يحتمل الصدق والكذب (2) لذاته (3)، ويسمى بالخبر (1) أيضاً.
(1) أي المركب التام. ويدعى قولاً. والحق أن القضية أعم من كونها لفظية كما نبه عليه الشارح نفسه في المعرفات. وكما هو معلوم من علم الكلام أن الكلام لفظى ونفسى. وعليه فالقضية إما لفظية أي ملفوظة أو عقليه أى معقولة. وهما مشتركان فى جنس واحد وهو القول، إذ كلاهما من مصاديقه، وهو مقول عليهما في جواب ما هو بحسب الشركة فقط فيكون جنساً لهما، كما عرفته فى باب المعرفات ومباديها. (2) خرج به ما لا يحتمل الصدق والكذب، أى ما لا يصح أن يقال لقائله إنك صادق أو كاذب، كالمركبات الإنشائية، سواء الطلبية كالأمر والنهي، أو غير الطلبية كالقسم وأفعال المدح والذم، وصيغ العقود كبعت واشتريت. فكل منها ليس بقضيّة بل هو من باب التصورات الساذجة التي لا حكم معها. فلها عندنا حكم المفردات. تدبّر. واعلم أنه وإن كان كل مركب كلاماً عند النحاة فلا يلزم أن يكون قضية عند أهل هذا الفن. ثم خرج بقيد الصدق والكذب وكذلك بقيد التمام الذى أضمره في كلامه المركبات التقييدية بالوصف أو الإضافة لأنها عارية عن النسبة التى هى مورد التصديق والتكذيب، كما مر.
(3) خرج به ما احتمل الصدق لا لذاته، كما سيبينه.
What admits truth for its own essence is called among them a proposition (qadiyya) and a report (khabar). I say: Proposition is derived from al-qada' which is judgment, because it contains it. And the purpose of the rules of propositions is contradiction and conversion. As for the definition of proposition, it is the expression (1) that admits truth and falsity (2) inherently (3), and it is also called a report (khabar). (1) i.e., the complete composite, also called a saying (qawl). The truth is that proposition is more general than being verbal, as the commentator himself noted in the definitions. And as is known from kalam, speech is verbal and psychical. Accordingly, proposition is either verbal, i.e., uttered, or mental, i.e., intellectual. Both share one genus which is saying (qawl), since both are instances of it, and it is predicated on them in response to 'what is it' solely by way of sharing, so it becomes a genus for them, as you learned in the chapter on definitions and their principles. (2) By this is excluded what does not admit truth and falsity, i.e., what cannot be said to its utterer 'you are truthful or lying', such as constative sentences, whether imperative like command and prohibition, or non-imperative like oath and verbs of praise and blame, and contract formulas like 'I sold' and 'I bought'. None of these is a proposition, but rather belongs to simple concepts without judgment. In our view, they have the status of individual terms. Ponder. And know that although every composite is speech for grammarians, it does not necessarily follow that it is a proposition for the practitioners of this art. Then by the condition of truth and falsity, as well as the condition of completeness that he implied in his speech, restrictive compositions by adjective or annexation are excluded, because they lack the relation that is the locus of truth and falsity, as previously mentioned. (3) By this is excluded what admits truth not inherently, as will be explained.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of proposition (qadiyya) and its essential conditions. The author begins by stating the matn: 'What admits truth inherently is called a proposition or report.' Then he explains the derivation of the term and the purpose of studying propositions. The core definition: a proposition is an expression (lafz) that inherently admits truth and falsity. The commentary breaks down three conditions: (1) 'expression' means a complete composite (al-murakkab al-tamm), which can be verbal or mental; thus mental propositions are included. (2) 'admits truth and falsity' excludes all non-assertoric sentences like commands, prohibitions, oaths, and performatives, because these cannot be evaluated as true or false. (3) 'inherently' excludes statements that admit truth but not by themselves, such as those dependent on external factors. The commentary emphasizes that only declarative sentences (akhbar) qualify as propositions, and that grammatically correct sentences that are not statements are not propositions in logic.

القضية (al-qadiyya)
الصدق والكذب (al-sidq wa al-kidhb)
اللفظ (al-lafz)
الخبر (al-khabar)

Key Takeaway
A proposition must be a complete expression that inherently admits truth and falsity; all other sentences fall outside logic's purview.

Classification of Propositions: Categorical and Conditional
Author classifies propositions by quality, quantity, and conditionality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any proposition as categorical or conditional, and identify its subcategories (quantified, unquantified, conjunctive, disjunctive, and subtypes).

Core Idea
All propositions are either categorical judgments or conditional connections.

This lesson introduces the fundamental classification of propositions (qadaya) in Islamic logic. Understanding this classification is crucial because every syllogism is built from propositions, and knowing their types is the first step in evaluating arguments. The matn presents a concise taxonomy that serves as a memorization anchor for all subsequent logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main division
Locate the two primary types: hamliyya (categorical) and shartiyya (conditional). In the matn, 'then propositions according to them are two divisions: conditional and categorical'.
This is the highest-level split.
2
Classify categorical propositions by quantity and quality
Categorical propositions divide into universal (kulliyya) and individual (shakhsiyya), and then into quantified (musawwar) or unquantified (muhmal). The quantifier (sur) has four types: 'all', 'some', 'none', 'not some'. Each can be affirmative or negative, producing eight types.
Memorize the four quantifiers: كل، بعض، لا شيء، ليس بعض.
3
Classify conditional propositions into conjunctive and disjunctive
Conditional propositions split into muttasila (conjunctive) where the parts imply coexistence (talazum), and munfasila (disjunctive) where they imply mutual exclusivity (tanafur). For disjunctive, note the three subtypes: man' al-jam' (both cannot be true), man' al-khulu (both cannot be false), and man' al-jam' wa al-khulu (the real exclusive).
Understand the difference between talazum and tanafur.
4
Apply the classification to sample propositions
Take a given proposition and trace through the tree: Is it categorical or conditional? If categorical, what is its quantifier and quality? If conditional, is it conjunctive or disjunctive? For disjunctive, which subtype?
Practice with examples from Islamic sciences.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ القَضَايَا عِنْدَهُم قِسْمانِ شَرْطِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيةٌ وَالتّان
وَالثَّاني كُلِّيَّةٌ شَخْصِيَّةٌ وَالأَوَّلُ إِمَّا مُسَوَّرٌ وَإِمَّا مُهْمَلُ
وَالسُّورُ كُلِّيَّاً وَجُزئياً يُرَى وَأَرْبَعٌ أَقْسَامُهُ حَيْثُ جَرى
إِمَّا بِكُلِّ أَوْ بِبَعْضٍ أَوْ بلا شَيْءَ وَلَيْسَ بَعْضُ أَوْ شَبَهِ جَلا
وَكُلَّها مُوجِبَةٌ وَسالِبَــة فَهيَ إذا إلى الثمانِ آيبَةُ
والأوّل الموضوع بالحملية والآخِرُ المحمول بالسوية
وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْليقِ فيها قَدْ حُكُمْ فَإِنَّها شَرْطِيَّةٌ وَتَنْقَسِمْ
أَيْضاً إِلى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَةً وَمِثْلُها شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُنْفَصِلة
جُز آهُما مُقَدَّم وتالي أما بَيَانُ ذاتِ الاتصال ما أَوْجَبَتْ تَلازُمَ الجُرْأَيْنِ
وذات الانفصالِ دُونَ مَيْنِ ما أَوْجَبَتْ تَنَافُراً بينها
أقسامها ثلاثَةٌ فَلْتَعْلَا
مانِعُ جَمْع أَوْ خُلُو أَوْ هُمَا وَهْوَ الحَقِيقِيُّ الأَخَصُّ فَاعْلَما
Then propositions according to them are two divisions: conditional and categorical, and the two. The second is universal and individual, and the first is either quantified or unquantified. The quantifier is seen as universal or particular, and its divisions are four as it occurs: either by 'all', or 'some', or nothing, and 'not some' or its likeness are clear. And all of them are affirmative and negative, so they return to eight. The first is the subject in the categorical, and the last is the predicate equally. And if judgment is made in it upon connection, then it is conditional, and it also divides into conditional conjunctive and the like is conditional disjunctive. Each part of them is antecedent and consequent. As for the explanation of the conjunctive, it necessitates the coexistence of the two parts; and the disjunctive, without flaw, necessitates mutual exclusivity between them. Its divisions are three, so know: mutually exclusive of combination, or of emptiness, or both, and it is the real most specific, so know.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of propositions. The matn states that propositions are primarily of two types: hamliyya (categorical) and shartiyya (conditional). The second (hamliyya) is then divided into kulliyya (universal) and shakhsiyya (individual), while the first (shartiyya) is either musawwar (quantified) or muhmal (unquantified). The quantifier (sur) has four varieties: 'all' (kull), 'some' (ba'd), 'nothing' (i.e., none), and 'not some' (laysa ba'd) or its equivalent. All propositions are also either affirmative (mujiba) or negative (saliba), so when multiplied by quantity, they yield eight types. In a categorical proposition, the first part is the subject (mawdu') and the last is the predicate (mahmul) equally. If the judgment involves a connection (ta'liq), i.e., a condition, then it is conditional (shartiyya). The conditional is further divided into conjunctive (mutasila), where the two parts are linked by implication (talazum), and disjunctive (munfasila), where the parts mutually exclude each other (tanafur). The disjunctive has three subtypes: man' al-jam' (exclusive in combination), man' al-khulu (exclusive in emptiness), and both (man' al-jam' wa al-khulu), which is the real most specific (al-haqiqi al-akhass). This classification is foundational for syllogistic reasoning.

الشَّرْطِيَّة
المتصلة

السور
مانع الجمع
مانع الخلو

Key Takeaway
The foundational division of propositions into categorical and conditional, with their subdivisions, is the key to understanding syllogistic premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

(b) 'If it is a dog, then it is an animal.' (c) 'Either the prayer is valid or it is invalid.' (d) 'Some scholars are jurists.'
?

Propositions
beginner
10 min
The Core Definition of the Proposition (Qadiyya)
Author defines qadiyya and explains its essential conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of qadiyya and explain why it must be a complete composite that essentially can be true or false.

Core Idea
A proposition is a complete utterance that can be true or false by itself, regardless of who says it.

The author begins by defining the fundamental unit of logical reasoning: the proposition (qadiyya). This definition is the gateway to understanding all subsequent logical operations, from contradiction to syllogism. Pay careful attention to each condition: the utterance must be complete, and the capacity for truth or falsehood must be intrinsic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'qadiyya'. Notice its etymology from 'qada'' (judgment) because it includes a judgment.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'lafdh' (utterance), but clarified as a complete composite (murakkab tamm). Why must it be complete? Because only a complete composite can bear a truth value. The author notes that both verbal and mental expressions are included under this genus.
3
Extract the differentia
The differentia is 'admits truth and falsehood essentially (li dhatihi)'. This excludes performatives and incomplete constructions. 'Essentially' means disregarding any external evidence or context.
4
Test the definition's completeness
Does it include all propositions? Yes, both verbal and mental. Does it exclude non-propositions? Yes, as we will see in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: القضية مشتقة القضاء من وهو الحكم، لاشتمالها عليه. والمقصود من أحكام القضايا التناقض والعكس. وأما تعريف القضية فهي اللفظ (1) الذي يحتمل الصدق والكذب (2) لذاته (3)، ويسمى بالخبر (1) أيضاً.
(1) أي المركب التام. ويدعى قولاً. والحق أن القضية أعم من كونها لفظية كما نبه عليـه الشـارح نفسـه فـي المعرفات. وكما هو معلوم من علم الكلام أن الكلام لفظى ونفسى. وعليه فالقضية إما لفظية أي ملفوظة أو عقليه أى معقولة. وهما مشتركان فى جنس واحد وهو القول، إذ كلاهما من مصاديقه، وهو مقول عليهما في جواب ما هو بحسب الشركة فقط فيكون جنساً لهما، كما عرفته فى باب المعرفات ومباديها. (2) خرج به ما لا يحتمل الصدق والكذب، أى ما لا يصح أن يقال لقائله إنك صادق أو كاذب، كالمركبات الإنشائية، سواء الطلبية كالأمر والنهي، أو غير الطلبية كالقسم وأفعال المدح والذم، وصيغ العقـود كبعـت واشتريت. فكل منها ليس بقضيّة بل هو من باب التصورات الساذجة التي لا حكم معهـا. فلهـا عنـدنـا حكـم المفردات. تدبّر. واعلم أنه وإن كان كل مركب كلاماً عند النحاة فلا يلزم أن يكون قضية عند أهل هذا الفن. ثم خرج بقيد الصدق والكذب وكذلك بقيد التمام الذى أضمره في كلامه المركبات التقييدية بالوصف أو الإضافة لأنها عارية عن النسبة التى هى مورد التصديق والتكذيب، كما مر.
(3) خرج به ما احتمل الصدق لا لذاته، كما سيبينه.
والكلام كما هو معروف: إما خبر أو إنشاء، فالخبر ما مضى، والإنشاء ما لا يحتملها (2) كقولك [اضرب ] (3) ، والدعاء كقولك يا الله أعنّي]، والمركب تركيباً إضافيا كقولك [بيت زيد].
وأما ما يحتمل الصدق والكذب لا لذاته بل للازمه، كقولك [اسقني الماء] الدال على [أنا عطشان ] (4)، فليس قولك (اسقني ماء) خبراً كما هو ظاهر، بل لازمه هو الخبر المحتمل للصدق والكذب. ويشتمل التعريف أيضاً خبر الله تعالى وخبر رسوله عليه السلام، فإنه إنما قطع بصدقه بالنظر لقائله(5) لا بالنظر لذاته، ودخل أيضاً المقطوع بكذبه من الأخبار نحو الجزء أعظم من الكل، فإنه وإن قطع بكذبه فإنما هو لتحقق خلافه بضرورة العقل.
فالمقصود بقولنا ما احتمل الصدق لذاته، أي بقطع النظر عن الأدلة والبراهين أو الخارج، فأي خبر فهو محتمل لها بهذا الاعتبار، وأما القطع بمضمونه ، فإنما هو لأمور خارجة عن نفس كونه خبراً.
(2) أى الصدق والكذب. (3) أى كالأمر كقولك (اضرب). (4) دلاله (اسقنى ماء) على (أنا عطشان) بتوسط قرينة. وأما دلالته على (أنا) طالب الماء) أو (المخاطب مطلوب منه الماء) فبدونها، لأنّ كلّ إنشاء فإنه يستلزم لذاته خبراً من غير افتقار إلـى قرينة. وهـذا معنــى شـريف فاحرص عليه، فلعلک تحتاجه في علم الكلام. (5) أى لقيام الدليل القاطع على وجوب صدق الله تعالى واستحالة الكذب عليه، ووجوب صدق رسوله صلى الله عليه وسلّم واستحالة الكذب عليه. كما هو معلوم في محلّه.
I say: The proposition is derived from 'al-qada' which is judgment, because it includes judgment. And the purpose of the rulings on propositions is contradiction and conversion. As for the definition of a proposition: it is an utterance (1) that admits truth and falsehood (2) essentially (3), and it is also called khabar (report). (1) i.e., the complete composite, also called qawl. The truth is that the proposition is more general than being verbal, as the commentator himself pointed out in the definitions. And as is known from the science of kalam, speech is verbal and mental. Accordingly, the proposition is either verbal, i.e., uttered, or mental, i.e., conceptual. They share a single genus which is qawl, since both are instances of it, and it is predicated of them in answer to 'what is it' by way of commonality only, so it is a genus for them, as you learned in the chapter on definitions and their principles. (2) Excluded by this is what does not admit truth and falsehood, i.e., what it is not valid to say of its speaker 'you are truthful or lying', like performative composites, whether requestive like command and prohibition, or non-requestive like oath, verbs of praise and blame, and contract formulae like 'I sold' and 'I bought'. Each of these is not a proposition but rather belongs to bare concepts that have no judgment. For us they have the status of singular terms. Consider carefully. And know that even if every composite is 'speech' according to grammarians, it does not necessarily follow that it is a proposition according to the practitioners of this art. Then also excluded by the condition of truth/falsehood and the condition of completeness, which he implied in his words, are restrictive composites by description or annexation, because they are devoid of the relation (nisba) which is the locus of affirmation and denial, as previously mentioned. (3) Excluded by this is what admits truth/falsehood not essentially, as he will clarify. And speech, as is well known, is either khabar or insha'. Khabar is what [is subject to truth/falsehood], and insha' is what does not admit them (2) like your saying 'Strike!' (3), and supplication like your saying 'O Allah, help me!', and a composite with annexation like your saying 'Zayd's house.' As for what admits truth/falsehood not essentially but via its implication, like your saying 'Give me water' which indicates 'I am thirsty' (4), your saying 'Give me water' is not khabar as is apparent, rather its implication is the khabar that admits truth/falsehood. The definition also includes the speech of Allah Most High and the speech of His Messenger (peace be upon him), because certainty of their truth is only due to their speaker (5), not by virtue of itself. It also includes reports known to be false, like 'the part is greater than the whole', for even if one is certain of its falsehood, that is only because its opposite is established by intellectual necessity. So what we mean by our statement 'what admits truth/falsehood essentially' is: disregarding evidence and proofs and external factors; so any report is capable of this in this regard. As for certainty of its content, it is only due to matters external to its being a report itself. (2) i.e., truth and falsehood. (3) i.e., like a command, your saying 'Strike'. (4) The indication of 'Give me water' to 'I am thirsty' is by means of a contextual clue. As for its indication to 'I am a seeker of water' or 'the addressee is requested to provide water', that is without a clue, because every insha' necessarily implies a report essentially without needing a clue. This is a sublime meaning, so preserve it; perhaps you will need it in kalam. (5) i.e., because of the decisive proof establishing the necessity of truth for Allah and the impossibility of lying upon Him, and the necessity of truth for His Messenger and the impossibility of lying upon him, as is known in its place.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the proposition (qadiyya) in Islamic logic. The author begins by noting the etymology of 'qadiyya' from 'qada'' (judgment), and states that the study of propositions is primarily concerned with contradiction and conversion. Then he presents the formal definition: a proposition is a composite utterance (lafdh) that admits truth and falsehood essentially (bi dhatihi). He adds that it is also called 'khabar' (report). The commentary then expands on each part of the definition through a series of footnotes. The first footnote clarifies that 'utterance' here means a complete composite (murakkab tamm) which includes both verbal and mental expressions, as the proposition can be either spoken or conceptual. They share the genus 'qawl' (discourse). The second footnote explains the exclusion of compositions that do not admit truth/falsehood, such as performatives (insha'iyyat) — commands, prohibitions, oaths, and contract formulae. These are not propositions; they are mere concepts without judgment. Additionally, restrictive composites (like descriptive phrases or possessive constructions) are excluded because they lack the complete relational nexus (nisba) that is the locus of affirmation and denial. The third footnote prepares for a later discussion of what admits truth/falsehood accidentally. The author then deepens the discussion by distinguishing between khabar (report) and insha' (performative speech). He notes that some utterances, like 'Give me water', are not themselves reports but imply a report (the fact that the speaker is thirsty). He emphasizes that such utterances admit truth/falsehood only through their implication, not essentially. He also clarifies that the definition includes the speech of Allah and His Messenger because their truth is known by external proof (the character of the speaker), not by the utterance itself. Similarly, obviously false statements like 'the part is greater than the whole' are still propositions because they can be considered true/false in themselves, though reason decides otherwise. The key takeaway is that the essential possibility of truth/falsehood is considered independently of any external evidence or contextual factors.

Key Takeaway
A proposition is any complete composite that intrinsically can be true or false, whether spoken or thought.

Propositions
beginner
10 min
Excluding Performatives (Insha’) and Incomplete Composites
Author discusses exclusions from the definition of qadiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between propositions and non-propositions, specifically identifying performative utterances and restrictive composites as non-propositions.

Core Idea
Commands, questions, and other performatives are not propositions because they cannot be true or false.

The author now sharpens the definition by clarifying what is excluded. Many students mistakenly think every complete sentence in Arabic is a proposition. In logic, only statements that can be evaluated as true or false qualify. Commands, prohibitions, oaths, and even noun phrases like 'Zayd's house' are not propositions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the exclusion clause
The phrase 'admits truth and falsehood' excludes anything that cannot be called true or false. The text explicitly says 'what it is not valid to say to its speaker: you are truthful or lying.'
2
Identify categories of insha’
The text lists requests (command, prohibition), non-requestive insha’ (oaths, praise/derision verbs, contract formulae). Why are these not propositions? They perform an action rather than assert a claim.
3
Consider restrictive composites
Descriptive phrases like 'the wise man' or possessive constructions like 'Zayd's house' are incomplete because they lack the relational nexus (nisba) that can be affirmed or denied. They are not full propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والكلام كما هو معروف: إما خبر أو إنشاء، فالخبر ما مضى، والإنشاء ما لا يحتملها (2) كقولك [اضرب ] (3) ، والدعاء كقولك يا الله أعنّي]، والمركب تركيباً إضافيا كقولك [بيت زيد].

فالمقصود بقولنا ما احتمل الصدق لذاته، أي بقطع النظر عن الأدلة والبراهين أو الخارج، فأي خبر فهو محتمل لها بهذا الاعتبار، وأما القطع بمضمونه ، فإنما هو لأمور خارجة عن نفس كونه خبراً.
(2) أى الصدق والكذب. (3) أى كالأمر كقولك (اضرب). (5) أى لقيام الدليل القاطع على وجوب صدق الله تعالى واستحالة الكذب عليه، ووجوب صدق رسوله صلى الله عليه وسلّم واستحالة الكذب عليه. كما هو معلوم في محلّه.
I say: The proposition is derived from 'al-qada' which is judgment, because it includes judgment. And the purpose of the rulings on propositions is contradiction and conversion. As for the definition of a proposition: it is an utterance (1) that admits truth and falsehood (2) essentially (3), and it is also called khabar (report). (1) i.e., the complete composite, also called qawl. The truth is that the proposition is more general than being verbal, as the commentator himself pointed out in the definitions. And as is known from the science of kalam, speech is verbal and mental. Accordingly, the proposition is either verbal, i.e., uttered, or mental, i.e., conceptual. They share a single genus which is qawl, since both are instances of it, and it is predicated of them in answer to 'what is it' by way of commonality only, so it is a genus for them, as you learned in the chapter on definitions and their principles. (2) Excluded by this is what does not admit truth and falsehood, i.e., what it is not valid to say of its speaker 'you are truthful or lying', like performative composites, whether requestive like command and prohibition, or non-requestive like oath, verbs of praise and blame, and contract formulae like 'I sold' and 'I bought'. Each of these is not a proposition but rather belongs to bare concepts that have no judgment. For us they have the status of singular terms. Consider carefully. And know that even if every composite is 'speech' according to grammarians, it does not necessarily follow that it is a proposition according to the practitioners of this art. Then also excluded by the condition of truth/falsehood and the condition of completeness, which he implied in his words, are restrictive composites by description or annexation, because they are devoid of the relation (nisba) which is the locus of affirmation and denial, as previously mentioned. (3) Excluded by this is what admits truth/falsehood not essentially, as he will clarify. And speech, as is well known, is either khabar or insha'. Khabar is what [is subject to truth/falsehood], and insha' is what does not admit them (2) like your saying 'Strike!' (3), and supplication like your saying 'O Allah, help me!', and a composite with annexation like your saying 'Zayd's house.' As for what admits truth/falsehood not essentially but via its implication, like your saying 'Give me water' which indicates 'I am thirsty' (4), your saying 'Give me water' is not khabar as is apparent, rather its implication is the khabar that admits truth/falsehood. The definition also includes the speech of Allah Most High and the speech of His Messenger (peace be upon him), because certainty of their truth is only due to their speaker (5), not by virtue of itself. It also includes reports known to be false, like 'the part is greater than the whole', for even if one is certain of its falsehood, that is only because its opposite is established by intellectual necessity. So what we mean by our statement 'what admits truth/falsehood essentially' is: disregarding evidence and proofs and external factors; so any report is capable of this in this regard. As for certainty of its content, it is only due to matters external to its being a report itself. (2) i.e., truth and falsehood. (3) i.e., like a command, your saying 'Strike'. (4) The indication of 'Give me water' to 'I am thirsty' is by means of a contextual clue. As for its indication to 'I am a seeker of water' or 'the addressee is requested to provide water', that is without a clue, because every insha' necessarily implies a report essentially without needing a clue. This is a sublime meaning, so preserve it; perhaps you will need it in kalam. (5) i.e., because of the decisive proof establishing the necessity of truth for Allah and the impossibility of lying upon Him, and the necessity of truth for His Messenger and the impossibility of lying upon him, as is known in its place.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the proposition (qadiyya) in Islamic logic. The author begins by noting the etymology of 'qadiyya' from 'qada'' (judgment), and states that the study of propositions is primarily concerned with contradiction and conversion. Then he presents the formal definition: a proposition is a composite utterance (lafdh) that admits truth and falsehood essentially (bi dhatihi). He adds that it is also called 'khabar' (report). The commentary then expands on each part of the definition through a series of footnotes. The first footnote clarifies that 'utterance' here means a complete composite (murakkab tamm) which includes both verbal and mental expressions, as the proposition can be either spoken or conceptual. They share the genus 'qawl' (discourse). The second footnote explains the exclusion of compositions that do not admit truth/falsehood, such as performatives (insha'iyyat) — commands, prohibitions, oaths, and contract formulae. These are not propositions; they are mere concepts without judgment. Additionally, restrictive composites (like descriptive phrases or possessive constructions) are excluded because they lack the complete relational nexus (nisba) that is the locus of affirmation and denial. The third footnote prepares for a later discussion of what admits truth/falsehood accidentally. The author then deepens the discussion by distinguishing between khabar (report) and insha' (performative speech). He notes that some utterances, like 'Give me water', are not themselves reports but imply a report (the fact that the speaker is thirsty). He emphasizes that such utterances admit truth/falsehood only through their implication, not essentially. He also clarifies that the definition includes the speech of Allah and His Messenger because their truth is known by external proof (the character of the speaker), not by the utterance itself. Similarly, obviously false statements like 'the part is greater than the whole' are still propositions because they can be considered true/false in themselves, though reason decides otherwise. The key takeaway is that the essential possibility of truth/falsehood is considered independently of any external evidence or contextual factors.

Propositions
intermediate
12 min
Essential vs. Accidental Truth/Falsity: Divine Speech and Self-Evident Falsehoods
Author discusses essential vs. accidental truth and inclusion of divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why divine speech and obviously false statements are still considered propositions, and differentiate between essential and accidental truth/falsity.

Core Idea
Truth and falsehood belong to the proposition itself; certainty comes from external proof, not from the proposition’s nature.

The author addresses potential complications: if a statement is known to be true (e.g., Allah’s words) or known to be false (e.g., 'the part is greater than the whole'), is it still a proposition? Yes, because the capacity for truth/falsehood is considered in itself, independent of any external evidence. Similarly, performatives like 'Give me water' imply an underlying report ('I am thirsty'), but the performative itself is not a proposition.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish essential vs. accidental
Essential (li dhatihi) means disregarding all external factors. Accidental means admitting truth/falsehood via implication or external proof.
2
Apply to divine speech
Allah’s words are true because of His nature, not because the words themselves are incapable of falsehood; they still are propositions because they could be false considered in themselves.
3
Apply to obviously false statements
'The part is greater than the whole' is false by reason, but considered in itself it could be true; hence it is a proposition.
4
Understand implied reports
Insha’ like commands imply a report (e.g., 'I need water'), but the command itself is not a report; only the implication is.

أقول: القضية مشتقة القضاء من وهو الحكم، لاشتمالها عليه. والمقصود من أحكام القضايا التناقض والعكس. وأما تعريف القضية فهي اللفظ (1) الذي يحتمل الصدق والكذب (2) لذاته (3)، ويسمى بالخبر (1) أيضاً.
(1) أي المركب التام. ويدعى قولاً. والحق أن القضية أعم من كونها لفظية كما نبه عليـه الشـارح نفسـه فـي المعرفات. وكما هو معلوم من علم الكلام أن الكلام لفظى ونفسى. وعليه فالقضية إما لفظية أي ملفوظة أو عقليه أى معقولة. وهما مشتركان فى جنس واحد وهو القول، إذ كلاهما من مصاديقه، وهو مقول عليهما في جواب ما هو بحسب الشركة فقط فيكون جنساً لهما، كما عرفته فى باب المعرفات ومباديها. (3) خرج به ما احتمل الصدق لا لذاته، كما سيبينه.
والكلام كما هو معروف: إما خبر أو إنشاء، فالخبر ما مضى، والإنشاء ما لا يحتملها (2) كقولك [اضرب ] (3) ، والدعاء كقولك يا الله أعنّي]، والمركب تركيباً إضافيا كقولك [بيت زيد].
وأما ما يحتمل الصدق والكذب لا لذاته بل للازمه، كقولك [اسقني الماء] الدال على [أنا عطشان ] (4)، فليس قولك (اسقني ماء) خبراً كما هو ظاهر، بل لازمه هو الخبر المحتمل للصدق والكذب. ويشتمل التعريف أيضاً خبر الله تعالى وخبر رسوله عليه السلام، فإنه إنما قطع بصدقه بالنظر لقائله(5) لا بالنظر لذاته، ودخل أيضاً المقطوع بكذبه من الأخبار نحو الجزء أعظم من الكل، فإنه وإن قطع بكذبه فإنما هو لتحقق خلافه بضرورة العقل.
فالمقصود بقولنا ما احتمل الصدق لذاته، أي بقطع النظر عن الأدلة والبراهين أو الخارج، فأي خبر فهو محتمل لها بهذا الاعتبار، وأما القطع بمضمونه ، فإنما هو لأمور خارجة عن نفس كونه خبراً.
(2) أى الصدق والكذب. (3) أى كالأمر كقولك (اضرب). (4) دلاله (اسقنى ماء) على (أنا عطشان) بتوسط قرينة. وأما دلالته على (أنا) طالب الماء) أو (المخاطب مطلوب منه الماء) فبدونها، لأنّ كلّ إنشاء فإنه يستلزم لذاته خبراً من غير افتقار إلـى قرينة. وهـذا معنــى شـريف فاحرص عليه، فلعلک تحتاجه في علم الكلام. (5) أى لقيام الدليل القاطع على وجوب صدق الله تعالى واستحالة الكذب عليه، ووجوب صدق رسوله صلى الله عليه وسلّم واستحالة الكذب عليه. كما هو معلوم في محلّه.
I say: The proposition is derived from 'al-qada' which is judgment, because it includes judgment. And the purpose of the rulings on propositions is contradiction and conversion. As for the definition of a proposition: it is an utterance (1) that admits truth and falsehood (2) essentially (3), and it is also called khabar (report). (1) i.e., the complete composite, also called qawl. The truth is that the proposition is more general than being verbal, as the commentator himself pointed out in the definitions. And as is known from the science of kalam, speech is verbal and mental. Accordingly, the proposition is either verbal, i.e., uttered, or mental, i.e., conceptual. They share a single genus which is qawl, since both are instances of it, and it is predicated of them in answer to 'what is it' by way of commonality only, so it is a genus for them, as you learned in the chapter on definitions and their principles. Each of these is not a proposition but rather belongs to bare concepts that have no judgment. For us they have the status of singular terms. Consider carefully. And know that even if every composite is 'speech' according to grammarians, it does not necessarily follow that it is a proposition according to the practitioners of this art. Propositions
beginner

Categorical vs. Conditional Propositions
Author distinguishes categorical from conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a categorical proposition from a conditional proposition by identifying whether the judgment is directly on a subject-predicate pair or on a connection between two statements.

Core Idea
Categorical propositions predicate directly; conditional propositions predicate a connection.

The matn opens by dividing propositions into two fundamental types: categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). This division hinges on the nature of the judgment—whether it directly links a subject and predicate, or links two entire statements. Understanding this division is the first step in mastering the structure of arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the judgment type
Read the proposition and determine if the judgment is simply about a subject bearing a predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is standing') or about a connection between two statements (e.g., 'If Zayd is standing, then he is moving').
2
Look for the subject and predicate
In a categorical proposition, there is one subject and one predicate. In a conditional, there are two parts (antecedent and consequent) connected by a relation.
3
Apply the matn's rule
The matn says: 'If there is a judgment upon a connection, then it is conditional.' If the judgment is not upon a connection, it is categorical.
4
Test with Islamic examples
Consider 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' – this is categorical. Consider 'If you travel, then shorten the prayer' – this is conditional. Practice distinguishing these.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكُلَّها مُوجِبَةٌ وَسالِبَــة فَهيَ إذا إلى الثمانِ آيبَةُ والأوّل الموضوع بالحملية والآخِرُ المحمول بالسوية وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْليقِ فيها قَدْ حُكُمْ فَإِنَّها شَرْطِيَّةٌ وَتَنْقَسِمْ أَيْضاً إِلى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَةً وَمِثْلُها شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُنْفَصِلة جُز آهُما مُقَدَّم وتالي أما بَيَانُ ذاتِ الاتصال ما أَوْجَبَتْ تَلازُمَ الجُرْأَيْنِ وذات الانفصالِ دُونَ مَيْنِ ما أَوْجَبَتْ تَنَافُراً بينها أقسامها ثلاثَةٌ فَلْتَعْلَا مانِعُ جَمْع أَوْ خُلُو أَوْ هُمَا وَهْوَ الحَقِيقِيُّ الأَخَصُّ فَاعْلَما
أقسام القضية: (6)
(1) القضية تسمى خبراً من حيث اشتمالها على الصدق والكذب، ومن حيث إفادتها للحكم إخباراً، ومـن حيـث اشتمالها على الحكم قضيّة، لأن القضية مأخوذ من القضاء بمعنى الحكم، ولعلها من تسمية الكل باسم الجزء لما أن القضية تتضمن الحكم، وتسمى من حيث كونها جزء الدليل مقدمه، ومن حيث تطلب بالدليل مطلوباً، ومن حيث تحصل بالدليل نتيجة، ومن حيث إنها تقع في العلوم ويسأل عنها مسألة، فالذات واحدة واختلاف العبارات عليها باختلاف الاعتبارات. فافهم.
(6) واذكر أن صدق القول وكذبه مطابقة حكمه للواقع وعدمها، ولا حكم كما ترى في أي مـن الإنشائيات والتقييديات. لأن الحكم إخبار عن الواقع في نفس الأمر بمنتسبين بنسبة خبريه تامه، هذان المنتسبان ويسمى طرفي النسبة، والموضوع والمحمول أيضاً، والمحكوم عليه وبه وغير ذلك. وتسمى هذه النسبة بالحكميـة لأنها مورد الحكم. وهى ليست بمتحققة في الإنشائيات والتقييديات كما تكلمنا عليه غير مرة. ولاحظ أن غير المركبات الخبرية ليست مخبرة عن الواقع في نفس الأمر. ويسمى إخبــار الواقع أداء لـه، ولا أداء فــي الإنشائيات والتقييديات فاحفظ أن الحكم أداء للواقع فى نفس الأمر من طرفي النسبة، وهما الثبوت والوقوع في القضية الموجبة، والانتفاء واللاوقوع في السالبة. فتحصل عندك إن شاء الله أن القضية قول مركب تـام - أى يحسن السكوت عليه - يحتمل الصدق والكذب لذاته. وعرفت أن لها طرفين منتسبين بنسبه حكميّة. فی
And all of them are affirmative and negative, so they return [in combination] to eight [types]. The first [part] is the subject in the categorical, and the last [part] is the predicate equally. And if there is a judgment upon a connection, then it is conditional, and it also divides into conjunctive conditional and disjunctive conditional, each of whose parts is called antecedent and consequent. As for the description of the conjunctive: it necessitates the conjunction of the two parts; and the disjunctive: without exception, it necessitates incompatibility between them. Its divisions are three, so let it be known: preventer of both together, or preventer of both empty, or both together; and this latter is the real and most specific, so know [this].
The divisions of the proposition: (6) (1) The proposition is called 'khabar' in terms of its comprising truth and falsehood, and in terms of its conveying judgment as a report, and in terms of its comprising judgment it is 'qadiyya', because 'qadiyya' is derived from 'qada'' meaning judgment; perhaps it is naming the whole after the part because the proposition entails the judgment. And it is called 'muqaddima' (premise) in terms of being part of a proof, and 'matlub' (demand) in terms of being sought through a proof, and 'natija' (conclusion) in terms of being obtained through a proof, and 'mas'ala' (question) in terms of occurring in the sciences and being asked about. So the essence is one, and the different expressions for it vary according to different considerations. So understand. (6) And remember that the truth and falsehood of a statement is its judgment's correspondence with reality or lack thereof. And there is no judgment, as you see, in any of the performative and restrictive [composites]. Because judgment is informing about reality in itself of two things related by a complete reportive relation. These two related things are called the two sides of the relation, also subject and predicate, and the thing judged upon and the thing judged by, etc. This relation is called the 'judgmental' relation because it is the locus of judgment. And it is not realized in performative and restrictive composites, as we have discussed repeatedly. And note that non-reportive composites do not inform about reality in itself. Informing about reality is called 'performance' (ada') of it, and there is no performance in performative and restrictive composites. So preserve that judgment is a performance of reality in itself of the two sides of the relation, which are affirmation and occurrence in the affirmative proposition, and negation and non-occurrence in the negative proposition. So there results for you, God willing, that the proposition is a complete composite utterance — i.e., one upon which it is appropriate to remain silent — that admits truth and falsehood essentially. And you have known that it has two sides related by a judgmental relation.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions and delves into their components. The opening lines are from the matn (Sullam al-Mantiq), which states that all propositions are either affirmative or negative, and by combination with quantity (universal/particular) they become eight types. The matn then distinguishes categorical (hamliyya), where the judgment is simply about the subject's bearing the predicate, from conditional (shartiyya), where the judgment involves a connection between two statements. Conditionals are further divided into conjunctive (mutasila), where the two parts are linked by concomitance (talazum), and disjunctive (munfasila), where the parts are mutually incompatible (tanafur). The disjunctive has three subtypes: exclusive (mani' al-jam'), which prevents both from being true together; inclusive (mani' al-khulu), which prevents both from being false together; and the real disjunction (haqiqi), which prevents both together and both empty—this is the most specific of denial. The commentary then elaborates on the proposition's various names: khabar, qadiyya, muqaddima, matlub, natija, mas'ala—all referring to the same thing under different considerations. The author then reiterates the definition of truth and falsehood as correspondence of judgment with reality, emphasizing that only reportive composites (propositions) perform this judgment, while performative and restrictive composites do not contain a judgmental relation. He defines judgment as an act of reporting about reality concerning two related concepts (subject and predicate) with a complete reportive relation. He concludes that the proposition is a complete composite utterance that essentially admits truth/falsehood, consisting of two sides related by a judgmental relation.

Key Terms
حملية
شرطية

Key Takeaway
The primary division of propositions is into categorical (subject-predicate) and conditional (connection between two statements).
