The Principle of Taking the Certain in Indefinite Propositions
Author explains the ambiguity of indefinite propositions and the rule to rely on the certain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of taking the certain (al-muhqaq) to interpret indefinite negative propositions in Islamic theological and legal texts.

Core Idea
When a proposition is ambiguous, we affirm only what is certainly true and leave the doubtful aside.

Indefinite propositions lack a quantifier, making them ambiguous between universal and particular. This lesson introduces a key epistemological principle from Islamic logic: when confronted with such ambiguity, we adopt the interpretation that is certainly true—the particular—and disregard the mere possibility of the universal. This principle is essential for correctly interpreting ambiguous statements in the Qur'an, Hadith, and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the indefinite proposition
Look for a proposition without a quantifier such as 'every' or 'some'. In the source, the example is 'The animal is not a human' (الحيوان ليس بإنسان).
Indefinite propositions are called 'muhmala' (مهملة).
2
Recognize the ambiguity
Such a proposition can be interpreted as a universal negation (no animal is human) or a particular negation (some animals are not human). Both readings are possible in the abstract.
3
Determine which interpretation is certainly entailed by the other
If the universal is true, the particular is automatically true. But the particular does not imply the universal. Therefore, the particular negation is certain; the universal is doubtful.
This is a logical subalternation: universal entails particular.
4
Apply the rule: take the certain, leave the doubtful
We adopt the particular negation as the default interpretation because it is guaranteed to be true. We do not assume the universal unless there is additional evidence.
This rule is summarized as 'akhdh al-muhqaq wa tark al-mashkuk' (أخذ المحقق وترك المشكوك).
5
Practice with an Islamic example
Consider the indefinite negative: 'The hypocrite is not a believer.' Apply the same reasoning: the certain interpretation is that some hypocrites are not believers; the universal (no hypocrite is a believer) is possible but not certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أى ليست الفرسيه ثابته لكلّ فرد من أفراد الحيوان. وهذا هو المدلول المطابقى. فهذه الحالة لا يثبت معها أنّ المراد السلب الكلى أم السلب الجزئى، لأنك إذا تأملت العبارة التى تحتها خط وجدتها صادقة بأن لا تكون الفرسية ثابته لأحد من أفراد الحيوان، وهو السلب الكلى. وصادقة بأن تكون الفرسية ثابتة للبعض منسلبة عـــن البعض. وهو السلب الجزئي. وأيّاً ما كان فقد تحقق السلب الجزئى، لأنه إذا انسلب عن الجميع فقد انسلب عن البعض أيضاً. إذاً فليس كل يستلزم السلب الجزئى قطعاً، ويحتمل معه السلب الكلى. فاقتصرنا علـى الســلب الجزئى الأعم، أخذاً بالمحقق وتركاً للمشكوك. وستتكرر معنا في هذا الكتاب حالات مشابهة محتملة نأخذ فيها بالمحقق ونترك المحتمل. واعلم أن الأسوار الأكثر استخداماً هي ما أثبته الشارح في أمثلته وهی کل ولا شيء من وبعض، وليس بعض أو بعض ليس.
That is, 'horseness' is not affirmed for every individual of animal. And this is the literal signification. So this state does not establish whether the intended meaning is universal negation or particular negation, because if you consider the underlined expression you find it true in that 'horseness' is not affirmed for any individual of animal—that is universal negation; and true in that 'horseness' is affirmed for some and negated from some—that is particular negation. Whichever it is, the particular negation is certainly realized, because if it is negated from all, it is also negated from some. Therefore, universal negation necessarily entails particular negation, and with it universal negation is possible. So we restricted ourselves to the more general particular negation, taking the certain and leaving the doubtful. And similar probable cases will recur with us in this book; we take the certain and leave the probable. And know that the most commonly used quantifiers are those that the commentator established in his examples: 'every', 'none', 'some', and 'not some' or 'some not'.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage follows the examples of indefinite propositions. The author discusses the meaning of the indefinite negative example 'The animal is not a human.' The expression is ambiguous: it could mean universal negation (no animal is human) or particular negation (some animals are not human). The author argues that the particular negation is always realized because if the universal is true, the particular follows. However, the universal is not certain. Therefore, when interpreting indefinite negatives, we take the particular (partial) negation as the certain meaning, leaving aside the universal as doubtful. This is a methodological principle: take the certain (muhqaq) and leave the doubtful (mashkuk). The author notes that this principle will be used throughout the book. Finally, the passage lists the most common quantifiers (aswar): 'every' (kull), 'none' (la shay'a min), 'some' (ba'd), and 'not some' or 'some not' (laysa ba'd aw ba'd laysa). This helps in understanding logical notation.

Key Terms
مهملة (muhmala) — an indefinite proposition without a quantifier
المحقق (al-muhqaq) — the certain meaning that is definitely true
المشكوك (al-mashkuk) — the doubtful meaning that may or may not be true
سور (sawr) — a quantifier specifying the subject's extent

Classifying Propositions by Subject Type and Truth Conditions
Author presents a diagram of proposition types and explains truth conditions for universal and particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify categorical propositions into universal, particular, personal, and indefinite, and determine their truth conditions with respect to external referents.

Core Idea
The truth of a proposition depends on external referents, not just abstract concepts.

In this passage, the author presents a diagram that classifies categorical propositions based on their subject type—personal, indefinite, and universal—and then explains how the truth of universal and particular propositions relates to individuals in the external world. This classification is essential for understanding how logical rules apply to real objects, not merely mental concepts.

Guided Path
1
Parse the diagram structure
Identify the three main branches of the categorical proposition: personal (particular subject), indefinite (no quantifier), and universal (universal subject). Note the subdivisions under universal: quantified with universal or particular quantifier, each with positive and negative forms.
The diagram is fragmented in the source; reconstruct the tree mentally.
2
Understand the truth condition
The description of a proposition as universal, particular, or indefinite is based on how many individuals under the subject concept the predicate applies to. For example, 'Every human is mortal' is true for all human individuals.
3
Distinguish between concepts and referents
The author emphasizes that we deal with external individuals (musadiq), not abstract concepts (mafahim mujarrada). Logic connects to reality because its propositions are true of those external individuals.
4
Handle indefinite propositions
An indefinite proposition like 'The human is mortal' is ambiguous: it could mean all humans (universal) or some humans (particular). The certain meaning is the particular, as the universal includes the particular.

Classifying Categorical Propositions by Quantity
Author defines universal, particular, and indefinite propositions, and explains the rule for indefinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify categorical propositions by quantity and determine the logical force of an indefinite proposition.

Core Idea
An indefinite proposition is logically equivalent to a particular, not a universal.

This passage introduces the three types of categorical propositions based on quantity: universal, particular, and indefinite. The author also clarifies a common confusion: indefinite propositions lack a quantifier, but logicians treat them as particular. We will explore why and what that means for logical inference in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the definitions of كلية (universal), جزئية (particular), and مهملة (indefinite). Note the quantifiers: 'كل' for universal, 'بعض' for particular, and none for indefinite.
The subject of a universal proposition covers all individuals, particular covers some, indefinite is unquantified.
2
Understand the basis of classification
The classification is based on how many individuals (مصاديق) falling under the subject the predicate is affirmed of. Logic deals with external referents, not abstract concepts.
This shows logic's connection to reality, contrary to critics who claim it's purely imaginary.
3
Analyze the indefinite case
Consider the proposition 'Human is mortal' (الإنسان ميت). It can mean either 'Every human is mortal' (universal) or 'Some human is mortal' (particular). Since the universal includes the particular, the certain meaning is only the particular.
The indefinite is ambiguous between two readings, but only the particular is guaranteed.
4
Apply the rule
Logicians therefore say 'المهملة في قوة الجزئية' (the indefinite is in the strength of the particular). When you encounter an indefinite proposition, you can only conclude a particular claim with certainty.
This rule is crucial in Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh) when interpreting unqualified texts.

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of categorical propositions based on quantity: universal (like 'Every human is mortal'), particular (like 'Some human is mortal'), and indefinite (like 'Human is mortal'). The author explains that the classification depends on whether the predicate is affirmed of all or some of the individuals falling under the subject. He emphasizes that logic deals with external referents (individuals in reality), not abstract concepts, and therefore logical rules can be used to acquire real knowledge. Then he discusses the indefinite proposition: it can mean either 'every human is mortal' or 'some human is mortal'. Since 'some' is included in 'every', the certain meaning from 'Human is mortal' is only the particular, not the universal. Therefore logicians treat indefinite propositions as equivalent to particular propositions in strength. The first footnote refutes those who claim logic is purely imaginary by pointing out that its rules apply to external reality. The second footnote explains why indefinite is treated as particular: the universal implies the particular, but not vice versa, so the definite yield is the particular.

Key Terms
قضية كلية
قضية جزئية
قضية مهملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is universal.
B) It is particular.
C) It is both universal and particular.
D) It is neither.

Key Takeaway
An indefinite categorical proposition is logically equivalent to a particular proposition because only particularity is certain.

Definition of a Categorical Proposition
Definition of categorical proposition and its essential condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a categorical proposition from conditional propositions based on its defining feature: no conditional dependence for the predication.

Core Idea
A categorical proposition predicates directly without waiting for any condition.

This passage defines the categorical proposition and highlights its key property: it does not depend on any condition for the predication to hold. This distinction separates categorical propositions from conditional (shartiyya) or disjunctive (munfasila) propositions. Understanding this foundation is essential because only categorical propositions serve as premises in traditional categorical syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find where the author says 'كل قضية تحقق فيها حمل أمر على أمر من دون توقف على شرط'. This is the definition of a categorical proposition.
The phrase 'من دون توقف على شرط' is the differentia.
2
Extract the essential condition
The differentia 'without depending on a condition' means the predication is actual immediately from the structure of the proposition.
No external cause or condition is needed for the predication to hold.
3
Contrast with a conditional proposition
Take a conditional example: 'إذا كان الشيء خمراً فهو حرام' (If something is wine, then it is forbidden). Here the predication of 'forbidden' depends on the condition of being wine. This is not categorical.
In a categorical like 'الخمر حرام' (Wine is forbidden), the predication is direct.
4
Verify with your own examples
Think of a ruling from fiqh: 'الصلاة واجبة' (Prayer is obligatory). Is this categorical? Yes, because the obligation is affirmed without condition.
Test other rulings to see if they fit the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حقيقة القضية الحملية وأجزاؤها: كل قضية تحقق فيها حمل أمر على أمر من دون توقف على شرط في حصول الحمل ووقوعه، فإنها تسمى قضية حملية، لأن الحمل فيها متحقق بالفعل، ولا يتوقف تحققه على تحقق سبب ولا شرط غير نفس تركيب القضية الإسنادي. والقضيّة الحملية تتألف من ثلاثة أجزاء: الأول: الموضوع وهو المحكوم عليه بغض النظر عن ترتيبه وموقعه في الكلام، فإن أتى آخراً فلا يضر. فإن الاعتبار في القضايا إنما هو المعاني لا الألفاظ والأحكام أصلاً إنما هي المعاني لا الألفاظ، وحقيقة القول المنطقي إنما هو المعنى لا اللفظ. وسمي موضوعاً تشبيهاً له بشيء وضع ليحمل عليه كزيد من قولنـا [زيد قائم] أو [قام زيد]، فزيد] موضوع في المثالين، وإن كان مؤخراً في الثاني. (2) الثاني: المحمول وهو المحكوم به وسمي بذلك تشبيهاً له بالسقف المحمول على الجدار مثلاً. الثالث: النسبة أي ثبوت المحمول للموضوع كثبوت القيام لزيد مثلاً ، ويسمى اللفظ الدال على النسبة باسم الرابطة، والرابطة إما غير زمانية ككلمة [هو ] ) في قولنا [زيد هو قائم]، أو زمانيـة نحو [كان] في قولنا كان زيد قائماً، ولم يذكر المصنف الرابطة لعدم لزومها في القضية إذ كثيراً ما يستغنى عنها في لغة العرب بالإعراب والرابط اللفظي (4) (3)
(2) أى نُصِبَ ليُحمَلَ عليه غيره.
(3) في كلام الشيخ مسامحة أو قوله كلمه جرى على اصطلاح النحاة. لأن الكلمة عند المنطقيين هي الفعل عنـد النحاة. و[هو] التي هي الرابطة غير الزمانية إنما هي أداة على صورة الاسم. وكذلك القول في [کان] فهى رابطه زمانيّة على صورة الكلمة.
(4) أى أن لغة العرب لا يحتاج فيها إلى أداة تعبر عن النسبة بين المحمول والموضـوع كمـا هـو فـي اللغـة الإنكليزية مثلاً، فإن الخبر عندهم لا بد فيه من أحد أفعال الكينونة لربط المحمول بالموضوع. نحو (is) الرابطة الزمانية في قولهم: (He is a scholar . فإننا ننقلها إلى العربية بقولنا : هو عالم مع أن الترجمة الحرفية لهـا هي: هو يكون عالماً. ولكن هذه الترجمة غير صحيحة، كما أن ترجمتها إنه عالم غير صحيحة، لخلو عبارتهم هذه عن التوكيد. ولا أعلم في الإنكليزية رابطة غير زمانية.
The reality of the categorical proposition and its parts: Every proposition in which there is a predication of one thing upon another without depending on a condition for the occurrence of the predication is called a categorical proposition, because the predication is actualized, and its actualization does not depend on the actualization of any cause or condition other than the compositional structure of the statement. The categorical proposition consists of three parts: first, the subject (al-mawdu‘), which is that about which the judgment is made, regardless of its order and position in speech; if it comes at the end, it does not harm. For consideration in propositions is of meanings, not words; judgments are essentially about meanings, not words; and the reality of logical expression is meaning, not word. It is called 'subject' by analogy to something placed to be carried, like 'Zayd' in our saying 'Zayd is standing' or 'Standing is Zayd'; 'Zayd' is the subject in both examples, even if delayed in the second. (2) Second, the predicate (al-mahmul), which is that which is judged, and it is named thus by analogy to a roof carried on a wall, for example. Third, the relation (al-nisba), which is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject, like the affirmation of standing for Zayd, for example. The word indicating the relation is called the copula, and the copula is either non-temporal, like the word 'huwa' in our saying 'Zayd huwa qa'imun', or temporal, like 'kana' in our saying 'kana Zaydun qa'iman'. And the author did not mention the copula because it is not necessary in the proposition, as it is often dispensed with in the Arabic language through case endings and the verbal copula. (4) (3)
(2) Meaning 'it was set to have something else carried upon it.'
(3) In the author's speech there is some laxity or his saying 'kalima' follows the terminology of grammarians. Because 'kalima' for logicians is the verb for grammarians. And 'huwa', which is the non-temporal copula, is actually a particle in the form of a noun. Similarly for 'kana', it is a temporal copula in the form of a verb.
(4) That is, the Arabic language does not need a particle to express the relation between predicate and subject, as in English, for example. For their predicate must include one of the verbs of being to connect the predicate to the subject, like the temporal copula 'is' in their saying 'He is a scholar.' We translate it into Arabic as 'huwa ‘alim', while its literal translation is 'huwa yakunu ‘aliman'. But this translation is not correct, just as its translation 'innahu ‘alim' is not correct, because their expression lacks emphasis. And I do not know in English a non-temporal copula.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage defines the categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya) as one where a predicate is affirmed of a subject without any conditional dependence; the predication is actual simply by the structure of the statement. Then it lists the three essential parts: (1) the subject (mawdu‘), which is that about which the judgment is made, regardless of its position in the sentence; the focus is on meaning, not wording. (2) the predicate (mahmul), which is the judgment made about the subject. (3) the relation (nisba), which is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject, expressed by the copula (rabita). The copula can be non-temporal like 'huwa' or temporal like 'kana'. The author did not mention the copula as an essential part because it is often omitted in Arabic, where case endings suffice. The first footnote clarifies that 'subject' means that which is set to carry something. The second footnote corrects a terminological issue: the author used 'kalima' loosely; for logicians, 'kalima' means verb, but here it includes particles like 'huwa'. The third footnote contrasts Arabic with English, noting that English requires a copula (like 'is') whereas Arabic does not, and points out translation nuances.

موضوع
محمول
نسبة
رابطة

Components of a Categorical Proposition: Subject, Predicate, and Relation
Three components of categorical proposition: subject, predicate, relation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject, predicate, and relation in any categorical proposition and state the role of the copula.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition has a subject, a predicate, and a relation; the copula expresses that relation.

After defining the categorical proposition, the author breaks it down into its three essential parts: subject (mawdu‘), predicate (mahmul), and relation (nisba). He also discusses the copula (rabita) that expresses the relation and notes that in Arabic it is often omitted. Understanding these components is critical for analyzing the structure of arguments in logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Find the list of parts
The author says the categorical proposition consists of three parts: subject, predicate, and relation.
Look for the enumeration in the Arabic text.
2
Identify the subject
The subject (مَوْضُوع) is that about which the judgment is made, regardless of its position in the sentence. Example: in 'قائم زيد' (Standing is Zayd), Zayd is still the subject.
The passage stresses that logic cares about meaning, not word order.
3
Identify the predicate
The predicate (مَحْمُول) is what is judged about the subject. Example: in 'زيد قائم' (Zayd is standing), 'qa'im' is the predicate.
It is compared to a roof carried on a wall.
4
Understand the relation
The relation (نِسْبَة) is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject. It is expressed by the copula (رابطة) which can be non-temporal (like 'huwa') or temporal (like 'kana').
The copula is not an essential part because it is often omitted in Arabic.
5
Apply to an Arabic sentence
Take 'زيد عالم' (Zayd is knowledgeable). Identify subject (Zayd), predicate ('alim), relation (implicit). The relation is understood from the structure.
In Arabic, the copula is usually absent in present tense nominal sentences.

The Indefinite Proposition and Its Logical Force
Author explains that the indefinite proposition has the force of the particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an indefinite proposition and determine that its certain content is only particular, not universal.

Core Idea
An indefinite proposition never gives certain knowledge beyond a particular instance.

In logic, we often encounter propositions without explicit quantifiers. These are called indefinite propositions. The classical logicians established a crucial rule: an indefinite proposition is in the 'force of the particular' — meaning what it conveys with certainty is only a particular claim. This lesson unpacks why that is and how to apply this principle when analyzing statements in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the indefinite proposition
Look for a proposition that has no quantifier word like 'every' (kull) or 'some' (ba'd). For example: 'The human is mortal' is indefinite; 'Every human is mortal' is universal; 'Some human is mortal' is particular.
In Arabic, the lack of a quantifier (sūr) marks it as muhmala.
2
Compare indefinite with universal and particular
The indefinite is ambiguous: it could potentially be interpreted as universal or particular. But the logicians determine that its certain meaning is only the particular. Why? Because if the universal is true, the particular is automatically true, but the converse does not hold.
This is the logical subordination of the particular to the universal.
3
Apply the test: subordinate the indefinite to the universal
Test with an example: If 'Every human is mortal' is true, then 'Some human is mortal' is necessarily true. But if 'Some human is mortal' is true, 'Every human is mortal' may be false. Therefore, from an indefinite we can only be certain of the particular.
This is why the indefinite is said to be 'in the force of the particular'.
4
Use the rule in real Islamic texts
When reading a fiqh ruling or theological statement that is indefinite, do not assume it applies universally unless there is additional evidence. For instance, the Quran says 'وَأُمَّهَاتُكُمْ' (your mothers) without quantifier — it does not automatically mean all mothers, but must be interpreted in context.
This logical caution prevents overgeneralization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المهملة هو البعض لا الكل، ولذلك يقول المناطقة [إن المهملة في قوة الجزئية أي إن ما تفيدنا إياه على سبيل القطع إنما هو نفس المعنى المستفاد من القضية الجزئية فهي إذن في قوَّتها (1)

(1) لأنه إذا صدق أنّ كلّ إنسان ميت، فإنّه يصدق قطعاً أن بعض الإنسان ميت. ولكن العكس غير صحيح. فنأخذ الصادقة على كل حال، وهي القدر المقطوع به من تردّد المهملة بين المعنيين أعنى القضية الجزئية: بعض الإنسان ميت.
The indefinite [proposition] is the part, not the whole. Therefore the logicians say that the indefinite is in the force of the particular, meaning that what it conveys to us with certainty is only the same meaning derived from the particular proposition, so it is in its force. (1) Because if 'every human is mortal' is true, then it is necessarily true that 'some human is mortal'. But the converse is not true. So we take the true statement in any case, and it is the certain extent of the indefinite proposition's ambiguity between two meanings, i.e., the particular proposition: 'some human is mortal'.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage clarifies the logical status of the indefinite proposition (muhmala) – a proposition without a quantifier (like 'man is mortal'). The author states that it is not universal (al-kull) but rather particular (al-ba'd). Logicians treat it as equivalent to the particular in terms of certainty because it only guarantees a particular instance. The footnote gives an example: if 'every human is mortal' is true, then 'some human is mortal' is necessarily true, but not vice versa. Thus, the indefinite proposition can only be relied upon to the extent of a particular, not a universal. This is important for avoiding overgeneralizing from indefinite statements.

Key Terms
المهملة (al-muhmala)
الجزئية (al-juz'iyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is always false if not universal.
B) Because it only gives certainty of a particular instance, not universal.
C) Because it is equivalent to the universal when true.
D) Because it has no quantifier.

Key Takeaway
An indefinite proposition, lacking a quantifier, can only be relied upon for a particular truth, not a universal one.

Explain why it only gives certain knowledge about some instances, not all. Then state what additional evidence would be needed to apply the ruling universally.

The Categorical Proposition: Definition and Three Parts
Defines the categorical proposition and lists its three parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential parts of any categorical proposition and distinguish it from a conditional proposition.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition has a subject, predicate, and a relation linking them.

Before we can analyze arguments, we must understand their building blocks: propositions. This lesson introduces the categorical proposition (al-qadiyya al-ḥamliyya) — the most common type in logic — and its three indispensable parts. Once you master these, you'll be able to dissect any simple statement used in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: the categorical proposition (al-qadiyya al-ḥamliyya). The author describes it as a predication without condition.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
Genus: proposition (qadiyya). Differentia: predication of one thing on another without requiring any condition for that predication to occur.
3
List the three parts
The author states three parts: subject (al-mawḍū‘), predicate (al-maḥmūl), and relation (al-nisba). Note that the copula is the verbal expression of the relation.
4
Understand the subject's flexibility
The subject is that which is judged upon, regardless of its position. Even if it comes last, it remains the subject. The author gives the example 'Zayd is standing' and 'Stood Zayd' – both have Zayd as subject.

حقيقة القضية الحملية وأجزاؤها: كل قضية تحقق فيها حمل أمر على أمر من دون توقف على شرط في حصول الحمل ووقوعه، فإنها تسمى قضية حملية، لأن الحمل فيها متحقق بالفعل، ولا يتوقف تحققه على تحقق سبب ولا شرط غير نفس تركيب القضية الإسنادي. والقضيّة الحملية تتألف من ثلاثة أجزاء:
الأول: الموضوع وهو المحكوم عليه بغض النظر عن ترتيبه وموقعه في الكلام، فإن أتى آخراً فلا يضر. فإن الاعتبار في القضايا إنما هو المعاني لا الألفاظ والأحكام أصلاً إنما هي المعاني لا الألفاظ، وحقيقة القول المنطقي إنما هو المعنى لا اللفظ. وسمي موضوعاً تشبيهاً له بشيء وضع ليحمل عليه كزيد من قولنـا [زيد قائم] أو [قام زيد]، فزيد] موضوع في المثالين، وإن كان مؤخراً في الثاني.

The reality of the categorical proposition and its parts: Every proposition in which a predication of one thing on another is realized without being conditional upon a condition for its occurrence, it is called a categorical proposition, because the predication is actually realized and its realization does not depend on a cause or condition other than the very predicational composition of the proposition. The categorical proposition consists of three parts: First, the subject, which is that which is judged upon, regardless of its order and position in speech; if it comes last, it does not matter. For consideration in propositions is of meanings, not of words, and judgments are originally of meanings, not words, and the reality of logical discourse is meaning, not utterance. It is called 'subject' by analogy to something placed for something else to be predicated upon it, like Zayd in our saying 'Zayd is standing' or 'Zayd stood', so Zayd is subject in both examples, even if it is delayed in the second. (2) Second, the predicate, which is that which is judged, and it is so called by analogy to a roof carried on a wall, for example. Third, the relation, i.e., the affirmation of the predicate to the subject, like the affirmation of standing to Zayd for example, and the word signifying the relation is called the copula. The copula is either non-temporal, like the word 'huwa' in our saying 'Zayd huwa qa'im', or temporal, like 'kana' in our saying 'kana Zaydun qa'iman'. The author did not mention the copula because it is not necessary in the proposition, since it is often dispensed with in the Arabic language through 'i'rab and the verbal connection. (4) (3) (2) That is, it is set up for something else to be predicated upon it. It is a tool. (3) In the speech of the Shaykh there is leniency, or he said 'kalima' following the grammarians' terminology, because 'kalima' for logicians is the verb for grammarians. And 'huwa', which is the non-temporal copula, is only a tool in the form of a noun. Similarly for 'kana' – it is a temporal copula in the form of a verb. (4) That is, the Arabic language does not need a tool to express the relation between predicate and subject, as is the case in English, for example. For their statement must include one of the verbs of being to connect the predicate to the subject, like 'is', the temporal copula in their saying 'He is a scholar'. We translate it into Arabic by saying 'huwa 'alim', while the literal translation is 'huwa yakunu 'aliman'. But this translation is not correct, just as translating it as 'innahu 'alim' is not correct, because their phrase lacks emphasis. And I know of no non-temporal copula in English.

And the categorical proposition is called binary when there is no copula, since it consists of two parts, and when you mention the copula it is ternary, since it consists of three parts. (1) (1) If you mention the mode of the proposition, it is called four-part. It is not called five-part if the quantifier is added, because the meaning of the quantifier is not necessary for the proposition. And the mode of the proposition – it is the word indicating the manner of the proposition's relation in reality. The original [text] nor the commentary discussed it, so speaking about it is not appropriate for this book.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage defines the categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya) as a proposition where one thing is predicated of another without condition. It then lists its three parts: subject (al-mawdu'), predicate (al-mahmul), and relation (al-nisba) expressed by the copula (rabita). The copula may be non-temporal (e.g., 'huwa') or temporal (e.g., 'kana'). The author notes that the original matn omitted the copula because Arabic often uses 'i'rab (case endings) to indicate the relation. The footnote (3) clarifies a terminological issue: the logicians' use of 'kalima' (word) differs from the grammarians' use. Footnote (4) explains that Arabic does not require an explicit copula like English. Finally, the proposition is called 'binary' when the copula is omitted and 'ternary' when mentioned. Adding the mode (jiha) makes it four-part.

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition always consists of a subject, a predicate, and a relation linking them, regardless of word order or explicit copula.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'al-ṣalātu wājibatun' (prayer is obligatory). Identify its subject, predicate, and the relation. Is the copula explicit or implicit?
?
Consider the proposition 'kāna Allāhu ghafūran raḥīman' (Allah is forgiving, merciful). Identify the subject, predicate, and relation. Is the copula temporal or non-temporal?

Propositions
beginner

The Copula: Types and the Binary/Ternary Distinction
Explains the copula, its types, and the binary/ternary distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish non-temporal from temporal copulas and explain why Arabic propositions can be binary or ternary.

Core Idea
The copula expresses the relation; it can be temporal or non-temporal, and Arabic often omits it entirely.

Now that we know the three parts of a categorical proposition, we look at the copula – the word that signals the relation. The author explains that the copula can be non-temporal (like 'huwa') or temporal (like 'kāna'), and notes that Arabic frequently dispenses with it, making the proposition binary. This lesson clarifies these concepts and their significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the copula's function
The copula (al-rābiṭa) is the word that indicates the relation (al-nisba) between subject and predicate. It is not a separate part but the verbal expression of the relation.
2
Distinguish non-temporal and temporal copulas
Non-temporal copula: 'huwa' (he is) is used to express a timeless relation, e.g., 'Zayd huwa qā'im'. Temporal copula: 'kāna' (was) indicates time, e.g., 'kāna Zaydun qā'iman' (Zayd was standing).
Footnotes clarify that 'huwa' is a tool in noun form, and 'kāna' is a tool in verb form, following grammatical terminology.
3
Understand why Arabic often omits the copula
The author explains that Arabic uses case endings (i‘rāb) to indicate the relation, so an explicit copula is not needed. In English, a form of 'to be' is necessary.
Footnotes (4) compare Arabic to English, noting that English has no non-temporal copula.
4
Learn the binary/ternary distinction
When the copula is omitted, the proposition is called binary (consisting of subject and predicate only). When the copula is mentioned, it is ternary (three parts: subject, predicate, copula).
A note adds that mentioning the mode (jiha) makes it four-part, but that is beyond this lesson.

Propositions

Defining the Conditional Proposition and Its Two Types
Defines conditional and divides it into connected and disjunct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a conditional proposition, classify it as connected or disjunct, and explain the logical relationship between its parts.

Core Idea
The conditional proposition links two statements; the key is whether they can be true together.

In this lesson, we learn what makes a proposition conditional: it contains a conditional particle and links two propositions. The author defines it and then splits it into two types: connected (where both can be true) and disjunct (where they cannot). This distinction is fundamental for understanding how conditions work in logic and in Islamic legal theory, where conditional statements are used to formulate rulings.

Guided Path
1
Find the conditional particle
Look for words like 'if' (in Arabic 'in' or 'idha') or 'either/or' that signal a conditional link between two propositions.
The particle is what makes the proposition conditional.
2
Identify the two propositions
Split the conditional statement into the condition (antecedent, or muqaddam) and the result (consequent, or tali). Write them down separately.
These are the two 'sides' (tarafan) mentioned in the text.
3
Test compatibility
Ask: Can both propositions be true at the same time without contradiction? If yes, it is a connected conditional (muttasila). If no, it is a disjunct conditional (munfasila).
The text says the connected type has 'co-existence in existence' while the disjunct type does not.
4
Classify Islamic examples
Take a fiqh example: 'If the prayer is invalid, then wudu is required.' Both statements can be true simultaneously (prayer invalid AND wudu required), so it is connected. For a disjunct example: 'The month is either Ramadan or Shawwal.' They cannot both be true at the same time.
Use the criterion from the passage to classify.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضية الشرطية:
وهي

القضية التي يوجد فيها حكم بالتعليق، أي بربط إحدى القضيتين بالأخرى ، كقولنا كلما كان هذا إنساناً كان حيواناً ].
وتسمى قضية شرطية لاشتمالها على أداة الشرط.
وتنقسم القضية الشرطية إلى قسمين:
الأول: شرطية متصلة، كقولنا: كلما كان هذا إنساناً كان حيواناً]، وقولنا كلما كان الإنسان ناطقاً، كان الحمار ناهقاً، وسميت بالمتصلة لاتصال طرفيها أي اجتماعها في الوجود وعدم تعاندهما فيه، فليس وجود أحدهما مانعاً من وجود الآخر. الثاني: شرطية منفصلة، كقولنا [العدد إما زوج أو فرد]، فهذه القضية شرطية منفصلة لانفصال طرفيهـا وتعاندهما لعدم اجتماعها في الوجود

(2) عبّر بالربط لتدخل القضية المتصلة المتضمنة أداة شرط، ولتدخل القضية المنفصلة المتضمنة أداة عناد.
The conditional proposition: It is the proposition in which there is a judgment by conditioning, i.e., linking one of the two propositions to the other, like our saying 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal'. And it is called conditional because it contains a conditional particle. And the conditional proposition is divided into two parts: First, the connected conditional, like our saying 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal', and our saying 'Whenever a human is rational, a donkey is braying'. It is called connected because its two sides are connected, i.e., they co-exist in existence and are not contradictory in it, so the existence of one does not prevent the existence of the other. Second, the disjunct conditional, like our saying 'Number is either even or odd'. This proposition is a disjunct conditional because its two sides are separated and contradictory, not co-existing in existence. (2) He expressed it with 'linking' so that it includes the connected conditional containing a conditional particle, and includes the disjunct conditional containing a particle of contradiction.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional proposition (qadiyya shartiyya) as one that involves a conditional link between two propositions. It is so named because it contains a conditional particle (adāt al-sharṭ). It is then divided into two types: 1) The connected conditional (muttasila), where the two clauses are connected such that they can both be true together; examples given include 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal' and a trivial example 'Whenever a human is rational, a donkey is braying'. The latter shows that the connection does not require actual causality. 2) The disjunct conditional (munfasila), where the two clauses are mutually exclusive, like 'Number is either even or odd'. The footnote (2) explains that the author used 'linking' (rabṭ) in the definition to encompass both types: the connected conditional with its conditional particle and the disjunct conditional with its particle of contradiction.

شرطية متصلة
شرطية منفصلة

Key Takeaway
A conditional proposition is defined by a linking particle; its two types are distinguished by whether the two parts can be true together (connected) or are mutually exclusive (disjunct).

Definition and Parts of the Conditional Proposition
Author defines conditional proposition and its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a conditional proposition and identify its antecedent and consequent in any example.

Core Idea
A conditional proposition is a judgment of dependency between two propositions.

In Islamic logic, propositions are either categorical (hamliyya) or conditional (shartiyya). This lesson introduces the conditional type, which expresses a connection between two propositions. Mastering its structure is essential for understanding how arguments work in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the conditional particle
Look for words like 'if' (in), 'whenever' (kullamā), or 'either...or' (immā...aw) that signal a conditional relationship.
The particle determines whether it is connected or disjunctive, a distinction covered in the next lesson.
2
Identify the antecedent (muqaddam)
The first part of the conditional proposition, upon which the judgment depends. In 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal,' the antecedent is 'this is a human'.
3
Identify the consequent (tālī)
The second part, which is claimed to follow from the antecedent. In the example, the consequent is 'it is an animal'.
4
Confirm the judgment of dependency
The entire statement must assert a link between the two parts, not merely list two independent facts. The dependency is indicated by the conditional particle.
The commentary note (2) emphasizes that 'connection' (rabṭ) is used broadly to include both condition and opposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضية الشرطية: وهي القضية التي يوجد فيها حكم بالتعليق، أي بربط إحدى القضيتين بالأخرى، كقولنا كلما كان هذا إنساناً كان حيواناً. وتسمى قضية شرطية لاشتمالها على أداة الشرط. وتنقسم القضية الشرطية إلى قسمين: الأول: شرطية متصلة، كقولنا: كلما كان هذا إنساناً كان حيواناً، وقولنا كلما كان الإنسان ناطقاً، كان الحمار ناهقاً، وسميت بالمتصلة لاتصال طرفيها أي اجتماعها في الوجود وعدم تعاندهما فيه، فليس وجود أحدهما مانعاً من وجود الآخر. الثاني: شرطية منفصلة، كقولنا: العدد إما زوج أو فرد، فهذه القضية شرطية منفصلة لانفصال طرفيها وتعاندهما لعدم اجتماعها في الوجود. والقضية الشرطية تتألف من جزأين الأول يسمى مقدم والثاني يسمى تالي. (2) عبّر بالربط لتدخل القضية المتصلة المتضمنة أداة شرط، ولتدخل القضية المنفصلة المتضمنة أداة عناد.
The conditional proposition: It is the proposition in which there is a judgment of dependency, i.e., connecting one of the two propositions to the other, like our saying: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal.' It is called a conditional proposition because it includes a conditional particle. And the conditional proposition is divided into two parts: The first: a connected conditional, like our saying: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal,' and our saying: 'Whenever the human is rational, the donkey is braying,' and it is called connected because of the connection of its two sides, i.e., their coexistence in existence and the lack of opposition between them, so the existence of one does not prevent the existence of the other. The second: a disjunctive conditional, like our saying: 'The number is either even or odd,' so this proposition is a disjunctive conditional because of the disconnection of its two sides and their opposition due to their lack of coexistence in existence. And the conditional proposition consists of two parts: the first is called the antecedent and the second is called the consequent. (2) He expressed 'with connection' so that the connected conditional that includes a conditional particle would be included, and so that the disjunctive conditional that includes a particle of opposition would be included.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional proposition (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya) and explains its types. The author begins by stating that a conditional proposition is a judgment (hukm) of dependency (ta'liq), meaning that one proposition is linked to another. An example is given: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal.' The proposition is called 'conditional' because it includes a conditional particle (adāt al-shart). The definition is expanded upon by the note (2) which clarifies that the term 'connection' (rabṭ) is used broadly to include both the connected conditional (which contains a particle of condition) and the disjunctive conditional (which contains a particle of opposition, e.g., 'immā...aw'). Then the author divides the conditional proposition into two types. The first is the connected conditional (shartiyya muttasila), which asserts that the two sides (the antecedent and consequent) coexist in existence and do not conflict, meaning the existence of one does not prevent the existence of the other. Examples include: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal' (which shows a causal link) and 'Whenever the human is rational, the donkey is braying' (which is a coincidental connection, as explained later). The second is the disjunctive conditional (shartiyya munfasila), which asserts that the two sides are disjoined and opposed, meaning they cannot coexist in existence. Example: 'The number is either even or odd.' Finally, the passage notes that every conditional proposition consists of two parts: the first part is called the antecedent (muqaddam) and the second is called the consequent (tālī). This structure is essential for understanding how conditionals function in logical arguments.

Connected vs. Disjunctive Conditional
Author divides conditional into connected and disjunctive types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a connected conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive conditional (munfasila) based on whether the parts can coexist.

Core Idea
In a connected conditional the parts can both be true; in a disjunctive they cannot.

Not all conditionals are the same. Some assert that two things go together (connected), while others assert that they cannot both occur (disjunctive). This distinction is crucial for building correct arguments, especially in kalam where disjunctive syllogisms are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle
Connected conditionals use particles like 'if' (in) or 'whenever' (kullamā). Disjunctive conditionals use 'either...or' (immā...aw).
2
Check coexistence
Ask: Can both parts be true at the same time? If yes, it is a connected conditional. If they are mutually exclusive, it is disjunctive.
The passage uses examples: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal' (coexistence allowed) vs. 'The number is either even or odd' (mutual exclusion).
3
Classify the proposition
Based on the particle and the relationship, classify it as muttasila or munfasila. Provide a brief justification.

The Essence of the Connected Conditional: Coincidental vs Implicative
Author defines the connected conditional and divides it into lizumiyya and ittifaqiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a coincidental (ittifāqiyya) and an implicative (luzūmiyya) connected conditional, and identify the type of relation in the implicative (causality or correlation).

Core Idea
A connected conditional does not require a logical relationship—it can be based on mere coincidence.

This lesson explores the essence of the connected conditional. The author defines it as a proposition that necessitates co-occurrence between antecedent and consequent. Importantly, this co-occurrence may be due to a genuine relation or it may be purely coincidental. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing conditional statements in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Identify that a connected conditional requires co-occurrence (talāzum) between antecedent and consequent. The text states it 'necessitates co-occurrence and accompaniment between the two parts'.
2
Recognize the two types
The passage divides the connected conditional into coincidental (ittifāqiyya) and implicative (luzūmiyya). The example 'Whenever the human is rational, the donkey brays' is coincidental; 'If the sun is risen, daytime exists' is implicative.
Coincidental conditionals are valid even without a real relationship.
3
Analyze implicative relations
Implicative conditionals rely on causality or correlation. Causality has three forms: antecedent causes consequent, consequent causes antecedent, or both are effects of a common cause. Correlation (taḍāyuf) involves reciprocal definition, e.g., father and son.
The text explicitly mentions these three causality types and the correlation example.
4
Test your understanding
Given a conditional, determine whether it is coincidental or implicative. If implicative, identify the type of relation (causality – specify which form – or correlation).
Use the criteria from the passage: if there is a clear relationship, it is implicative; otherwise, it is coincidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حقيقة الشرطية المتصلة: هي القضية التي توجب التلازم والتصاحب بين الجزأين المقدم والتالي في الوجود لزوماً بأن كان لعلاقة أو بأن كان لا لعلاقة، وحتى لو كان اتفاقاً. (1) يمكنك أن تتصوّر الاتفاق من مثاله السابق: كلما كان الإنسان ناطقاً، كان الحمار ناهقاً، إذ تلاحظ أنه لا بين كون الإنسان ناطقاً وكون الحمار ناهقاً فلا تلازم بينهما. البتة. ولكن هذه الشرطية المتصلة صحيحة. لأنه لا يشترط لصحتها وجود علاقة بين اللازم والملزوم فيكفي فيه مجرد فرض صدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدم لمحض اتفاق أن جزأيها وجدا معاً كما ههنا. وهذا بخلاف ما لو قلت: إذا كانت الشمس طالعة، فالنهار موجود فإن ثمة علاقة واضحة بين اللازم والملزوم فالاتفاقية إذاً هي القضية التي حكم فيها بصدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدم، وليس لعلاقة توجب ذلك بل لمجرد صدقهما. وعليه فالشرطية المتصلة تنقسم إلى اتفاقية وقد عرفتها، وإلى لزومية وهي التي حكم فيها بصدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدم لعلاقة توجب ذلك، وهي ما بسببه يستلزم المقدم التالي كالعلية والتضايف. أما العلية فبأن يكون المقدم علة للتالي كقولنا: إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، أو يكون التالي علة للمقدم كقولنا: إن كان النهار موجوداً فالشمس طالعة. أو يكون المقدم والتالي معلولا علة واحدة. كقولنا: إن كان النهار موجوداً فالضوء منتشر على الأرض. وأما التضايف فبأن يكون المقدم والتالي بحيث يكون تعقل أحدهما بالقياس إلى الآخر، كقولنا إن كان زيد أباً لعمرو فعمرو ابنه. لأن تعقل كل واحد من المتضايفين يتوقف على تعقل الآخر. وهو ما يسمونه بالدور المعى.
The essence of the connected conditional: It is the proposition that necessitates co-occurrence and accompaniment between the two parts, the antecedent and consequent, in existence necessarily, whether there is a relationship or not, and even if it is coincidental. (1) You can conceive the coincidental from his previous example: 'Whenever the human is rational, the donkey is braying,' for you observe that there is no relation between the human being rational and the donkey braying, so there is no entailment between them at all. But this connected conditional is valid, because its validity does not require a relationship between the necessary and the necessitated; rather, it suffices that it is merely supposed that the consequent is true given the truth of the antecedent, due to sheer coincidence that its two parts co-occur as here. This is unlike if you said: 'If the sun is risen, then daytime exists,' for there is a clear relationship between the necessary and the necessitated. So the coincidental is the proposition in which it is judged that the consequent is true given the truth of the antecedent, not due to a relationship that necessitates that, but simply due to their truth. Accordingly, the connected conditional is divided into coincidental, which you have come to know, and implicative, which is the one in which it is judged that the consequent is true given the truth of the antecedent due to a relation that necessitates that, and it is that by virtue of which the antecedent entails the consequent, like causality and correlation. As for causality, it is that the antecedent is the cause of the consequent, as we say: 'If the sun is risen, then daytime exists,' or the consequent is the cause of the antecedent, as we say: 'If daytime exists, then the sun is risen,' or the antecedent and consequent are effects of a single cause, as we say: 'If daytime exists, then light is spread on the earth.' As for correlation, it is that the antecedent and consequent are such that conceptualizing one is relative to the other, as we say: 'If Zayd is the father of 'Amr, then 'Amr is his son,' because the conceptualization of each correlative depends on the conceptualization of the other. This is what they call the reciprocal entailment.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the definition of the connected conditional. The author begins by stating the essence (haqīqa) of the connected conditional: it is a proposition that necessitates co-occurrence (talāzum) and accompaniment (taṣāḥub) between the antecedent and consequent in existence. This necessity may be due to a relationship (lī-'alāqa) or not, and may even be coincidental (ittifāq). This crucial point is then elaborated in the commentary note (1). The commentator first revisits the earlier example: 'Whenever the human is rational, the donkey is braying.' There is no real relationship between these two propositions; the donkey braying does not logically follow from a human being rational. Yet the conditional is considered valid in logic because its validity does not require a genuine causal link. It is enough that we can suppose the consequent is true given the antecedent's truth, even if only by coincidence. In contrast, in 'If the sun is risen, then daytime exists,' there is a clear causal relation. Based on this, the connected conditional is divided into two sub-types. The first is the coincidental (ittifāqiyya), where the judgment of truth of the consequent given the antecedent is based on mere coincidence. The second is the implicative (luzūmiyya), where the judgment is based on a relation that necessitates the truth of the consequent from the antecedent. The commentator then explains the kinds of relations that create implication (luzūm). One is causality (al-‘illiyya), which can take three forms: (a) the antecedent is the cause of the consequent (e.g., sunrise causes daytime), (b) the consequent is the cause of the antecedent (e.g., daytime implies sunrise, as a sign), or (c) both are effects of a single cause (e.g., daytime and light on earth from the sun). Another relation is correlation (al-ṭaḍāyuf), where each term is understood in relation to the other, like fatherhood and sonship. This yields a reciprocal entailment (al-dawr al-ma‘ī). The passage thus clarifies that the connected conditional is broader than often thought, as it can be purely coincidental.

Key Terms
الشرطية المتصلة الاتفاقية
الشرطية المتصلة اللزومية
تلازم
علية
تضايف

B) Whenever a human is rational, the donkey brays.
C) If Zayd is 'Amr's father, then 'Amr is Zayd's son.
D) If the number is even, then it is divisible by two.

Key Takeaway
The connected conditional can be either coincidental or implicative; only implicative conditionals rely on a genuine relation like causality or correlation.

Essence of Disjunctive Conditional: Mani'at al-Jam' and Mani'at al-Khuluww
Author defines the disjunctive conditional and details its two types: exclusive disjunction and exhaustive disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between exclusive and exhaustive disjunctive conditionals (mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluww) and identify the logical structure of each.

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditionals split into two: one prevents both being true, the other prevents both being false.

In this passage, the author defines the disjunctive conditional and clarifies its two fundamental types. Understanding the difference between 'preventing togetherness' and 'preventing emptiness' is essential for evaluating disjunctive arguments in Islamic logic, particularly in legal reasoning and theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the essence of the disjunctive conditional
Notice that the author defines it as a proposition requiring opposition (tanāfur) and incompatibility (ta‘ādud) between the two parts, either in truth, falsehood, or both.
This sets the stage for the two subtypes.
2
Recognize the first type: māni‘at al-jam‘
This is an exclusive disjunction: the two sides cannot both be true, but they can both be false. The example given is 'This is either black or white' – black and white cannot coexist, but the object could be another color.
Also called 'preventing togetherness'.
3
Recognize the second type: māni‘at al-khuluww
This is an exhaustive disjunction: the two sides cannot both be false, but they can both be true. The example is 'This is either not-black or not-white' – it is impossible for something to be both black and white, but it can be both not-black and not-white (e.g., red).
Also called 'preventing emptiness'.
4
Understand the composition of each type
For māni‘at al-jam‘, the sides are the thing itself (al-shay') and something more specific than its negation (al-akhaṣṣ min al-naqīḍ). For māni‘at al-khuluww, the sides are the thing and something more general than its negation (al-a‘amm min al-naqīḍ).
This structural difference determines the logical properties.
5
Apply the distinction to Islamic arguments
Think of a legal ruling that is either obligatory or recommended – these cannot both be true of the same act (māni‘at al-jam‘). Or a penalty that is either a fine or imprisonment – these cannot both be avoided (māni‘at al-khuluww).
This shows the practical relevance.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حقيقة الشرطية المنفصلة: هي القضية التي اقتضت تنافراً وتعائداً بين الجزأين في الصدق أو في الكذب أو فيهما معاً. وأقسام الشرطية المنفصلة ثلاثة: الأول: مانعة جمع وهي قضية تمنع الجمع بين طرفيها فلا يجتمعان في الوجود ويمكن ارتفاعها، وتتركب من الشيء والأخص من نقيضه كقولنا: هذا الشيء إما أسود أو أبيض، فالسواد والبياض لا يجتمعان في المحل الواحد، ويمكن ارتفاعها بأن يوجد في المحل لون آخر. الثاني: مانعة خلو، وهي قضية تمنع الخلو عن طرفيها فلا يمكن ارتفاعها ويمكن اجتماعها وتتركب من الشيء والأعم من نقيضه كقولنا: هذا إما غير أسود أو غير أبيض، فيمكن اجتماعها في الأحمر ولا يمكن ارتفاعهما بأن يكون أسود وأبيض معاً. (2) قوله (من الشيء والأخص من نقيضه) مثل عليه بقوله: هذا الشيء إما أسود أو أبيض، الأسود هو الشيء، ونقيضه اللاأسود. فالأبيض أخص من نقيض الأسود وهو اللاأسود. لأن اللاأسود يعم الأبيض وجميع الألوان الأخرى باستثناء الأسود. (3) قوله (الشيء والأعم من نقيضه) ومثل عليه بقوله: هذا إما غير أسود أو غير أبيض. فالشيء هو غير الأسود، والأعم من نقيضه هو غير الأبيض. لأن نقيضه هو الأسود، وغير الأبيض أعم من الأسود، لأنه يصدق على الأسود وغيره. قوله فيمكن اجتماعهما في الأحمر) لأن الأحمر غير للأسود وغير للأبيض في آن واحد.
The essence of the disjunctive conditional: It is the proposition that requires opposition and incompatibility between the two parts in truth or falsehood or both. And the divisions of the disjunctive conditional are three: The first: preventing togetherness (man' al-jam') – it is a proposition that prevents the conjunction of its two sides, so they do not coexist in existence, and their removal is possible; it is composed of the thing and the more specific than its negation, like our saying: 'This thing is either black or white,' for black and white do not coexist in a single location, and their removal is possible (by there being another color). The second: preventing emptiness (man' al-khuluww) – it is a proposition that prevents the emptiness of its two sides, so their removal is not possible, and their conjunction is possible; it is composed of the thing and the more general than its negation, like our saying: 'This is either not-black or not-white,' so they can both be true in red, and their removal cannot be (by being black and white together). (2) His statement: 'from the thing and the more specific than its negation' – he gave as an example his saying: 'This thing is either black or white'; black is the thing, and its negation is not-black. So white is more specific than the negation of black, which is not-black, because not-black includes white and all other colors except black. (3) His statement: 'the thing and the more general than its negation' and he gave as an example his saying: 'This is either not-black or not-white'; the thing is not-black, and the more general than its negation is not-white, because its negation is black, and not-white is more general than black, because it applies to black and other things. His statement: 'so they can both be true in red' because red is not-black and not-white at the same time.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines the disjunctive conditional (shartiyya munfasila) and explains its two main types. The author begins by stating that the essence of the disjunctive conditional is that it requires opposition (tanāfur) and incompatibility (ta‘ādud) between the two parts, either in truth (ṣidq), falsehood (kidhb), or both. This means that the two sides cannot both be true, or cannot both be false, or both. The text then says the divisions are three, but only two are given; the third (which would be both preventing togetherness and emptiness) is missing from this passage. The first type is 'preventing togetherness' (man‘ al-jam‘), which is a proposition that prevents the two sides from being true together (they cannot coexist). For example: 'This thing is either black or white' – black and white cannot be true of the same object, but it is possible that the object is neither black nor white (e.g., red). So this is an exclusive disjunction (opposition in truth, but not exhaustive). The second type is 'preventing emptiness' (man‘ al-khuluww), which prevents the two sides from being false together; it is impossible for both to be false, but they can both be true. Example: 'This is either not-black or not-white' – if something is not-black, it could be white or other colors; if it is not-white, it could be black or other colors. But it can be both not-black and not-white simultaneously (e.g., red). However, it cannot be that it is both black and white at the same time. So this is an exhaustive disjunction (opposition in falsehood, not in truth). The commentary notes (2) and (3) explain the composition of these disjunctions. For type 1, the two sides are the thing itself (e.g., black) and a more specific form of its negation (e.g., white, which is a subset of 'not-black'). For type 2, the sides are the negation of the thing (e.g., not-black) and a more general form of its negation (e.g., not-white, which is more general as it includes black and others). These technical details help in constructing valid disjunctive propositions in logical arguments.

الأخص من النقيض
الأعم من النقيض

B) 'This thing is either black or white.'
C) 'Zayd is either in the house or in the mosque.' (assuming he can be in both)
D) 'This is either not-black or not-white.'

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive conditional is defined by opposition; its two main types are exclusive (cannot both be true) and exhaustive (cannot both be false).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Parts of a Conditional and the Two Types of Connected Conditional
Author defines conditional and its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent and consequent in a conditional proposition and classify a connected conditional as accidental or implicative.

Core Idea
A conditional proposition does not require a real causal link; even an accidental concurrence suffices for truth.

This lesson introduces the fundamental structure of conditional propositions (qadiyya shartiyya) and focuses on the connected conditional (shartiyya muttasila). You will learn that the truth of a conditional depends only on the truth of the consequent whenever the antecedent is true, regardless of any underlying relationship. This leads to a division into accidental and implicative conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of a conditional proposition
In the Arabic, locate the terms 'muqaddam' (antecedent) and 'tālī' (consequent) and note their order.
2
Understand the definition of the connected conditional
The passage states it necessitates concomitance between antecedent and consequent in existence, with or without a relation. Consider what this implies about the truth condition.
3
Examine the accidental example
Analyze the example 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' and see why it is considered true despite no logical connection.
The text says it is true because the consequent is true on the condition of the antecedent, even if by coincidence.
4
Distinguish accidental from implicative
Based on the criteria given (presence or absence of a relation that necessitates the implication), identify which type each example belongs to.
Accidental: no relation; Implicative: a relation such as causality or correlation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقضية الشرطية تتألف من جزأين الأول يسمى [مُقَدَّم] والثاني يسمى [التالي].
حقيقة الشرطية المتصلة:
هي القضية التي توجب التلازم والتصاحب بين الجزأين [المقدَّم والتالي ] في الوجود لزوماً بأن كان لعلاقة أو بأن كان لا لعلاقة، وحتى لو كان اتفاقاً.(1)
يمكنك أن تتصوّر الاتفاق من مثاله السابق [ كلما كان الإنسان ناطقاً، كان الحمار ناهقاً، إذ تلاحظ أنه لا بین كون الإنسان ناطقاً وكون الحمار ناهقاً فلا تلازم بينهما .البته. ولكن هذه الشرطية المتصلة صحيحة. لأنه لا يشترط لصحتها وجود علاقة بين اللازم والملزوم فيكفي فيه مجرد فرض صــدق التـالـي علـى تقـدير صدق المقدم لمحض اتفاق أنّ جزأيها وجدا معاً كما ههنا. وهذا بخلاف ما لو قلت: [إذا كانت الشمس طالعة، فالنهار موجود فإن ثمة علاقة واضحة بين اللازم .والملزوم فالاتفاقية إذاً هى القضيّة التي حكم فيها بصدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدم، وليس لعلاقه توجب ذلك بل لمجرد صدقهما. وعليه فالشرطية المتصلة تنقسم إلى اتفاقية وقد عرفتها ، وإلى لزوميّة وهى التى حكم فيها بصدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدم لعلاقة توجب ذلك، وهى ما بسببه يستلزم المقدم التالي كالعلية والتضايف. أما العلية فبأن يكون المقدم علة للتالي كقولنا: إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، أو يكون التالي علة للمقدم كقولنا: إن كان النهـار موجوداً فالشمس طالعة. أو يكون المقدم والتالى معلولا علة واحدة. كقولنا: إن كان النهار موجوداً فالضوء منتشر على الأرض. واما التضايف فبأن يكون المقدم والتالى بحيث يكون تعقل أحدهما بالقياس إلى الآخر، كقولنـا إن كان زيد أباً لعمرو فعمرو ابنه. لأن تعقل كل واحد من المتضايفين يتوقف على تعقل الآخر. وهو ما يسمونه بالدور المعى.
The conditional proposition consists of two parts: the first is called the antecedent and the second is called the consequent. The reality of the connected conditional: It is the proposition that necessitates the concomitance and accompaniment between the two parts [the antecedent and consequent] in existence, either with or without a relationship, even if it is accidental. (1) You can conceive the accidental from its previous example: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying.' You notice that there is no relation between man being rational and the donkey braying, so there is no concomitance between them. However, this connected conditional is correct because its correctness does not require a relation between the necessary and the necessary; it suffices merely the assumption of the truth of the consequent on the condition of the truth of the antecedent due to pure coincidence that its two parts occurred together, as here. This is unlike if you say: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists,' for there is a clear relation between the necessary and the necessary. So the accidental (ittiāqiyya) is the proposition in which the truth of the consequent is judged on the condition of the truth of the antecedent, without a relation that necessitates that, but merely due to their truth. Accordingly, the connected conditional is divided into accidental, which you have known, and implicative (luzūmiyya), which is the one in which the truth of the consequent is judged on the condition of the truth of the antecedent due to a relation that necessitates that, i.e., the reason by which the antecedent implies the consequent, such as causality and correlation. As for causality, it is that the antecedent is the cause of the consequent, as in our saying: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists,' or the consequent is the cause of the antecedent, as in our saying: 'If daytime exists, then the sun is rising,' or the antecedent and consequent are effects of a single cause, as in our saying: 'If daytime exists, then light is spread on the earth.' As for correlation (taḍāyuf), it is that the antecedent and consequent are such that the conception of one is in relation to the other, as in our saying: 'If Zayd is the father of 'Amr, then 'Amr is his son,' because the conception of each of the two correlatives depends on the conception of the other, which is what they call circular definition (al-dawr al-mu'ayy).
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage begins by defining the conditional proposition (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya) as composed of two parts: the antecedent (muqaddam) and the consequent (tālī). Then it delves into the connected conditional (al-shartiyya al-muttasila), which is a proposition that asserts a relation of concomitance between the antecedent and consequent, either with or without a causal relation. The author explains that even an accidental concurrence suffices for the truth of the conditional. This is illustrated with the example 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying,' where there is no logical connection, yet the conditional is considered true if both parts happen to be true. The passage then classifies the connected conditional into two types: accidental (ittiāqiyya) and implicative (luzūmiyya). The implicative type is further subdivided based on the nature of the relation: causality (al-'illiyya) and correlation (al-taḍāyuf). Causality includes three scenarios: antecedent as cause of consequent (sun rising causes daytime), consequent as cause of antecedent (daytime existence causes sun rising), or both are effects of a common cause (daytime and light spread). Correlation is when the conception of one depends on the other, such as father and son. The author concludes by noting that correlation is called circular definition (al-dawr al-mu'ayy).

مقدم
تالي
شرطية متصلة
اتفاقية
لزومية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what suffices for the truth of a connected conditional?
A) The truth of the consequent whenever the antecedent is true (even if accidental)
B) A causal relation between antecedent and consequent
C) Both parts being true at the same time
D) The antecedent being the cause of the consequent

Key Takeaway
The connected conditional is true whenever the consequent is true on the condition of the antecedent, regardless of whether there is a real relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Subtypes of Implicative Conditionals: Causality and Correlation
Details of implicative conditional subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three forms of causal implication and the correlational implication (taḍāyuf) in connected conditionals.

Core Idea
Implicative conditionals are grounded in causality or mutual correlation, providing the logical basis for many arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

This lesson explores the implicative connected conditional (luzūmiyya) in detail. The passage divides it into causality ('illiyya) and correlation (taḍāyuf). You will learn the three forms of causality—antecedent as cause, consequent as cause, and both as effects of a common cause—and how correlation works, as exemplified by the father-son relationship.

Guided Path
1
Recall the division of connected conditionals
From the previous lesson, remember that the connected conditional is divided into accidental and implicative. We now focus on the implicative type.
2
Identify the subtypes of implicative
In the Arabic, look for two categories: causality (al-'illiyya) and correlation (al-taḍāyuf). Note how the passage introduces them.
3
Analyse the three causal forms
Find the examples: (1) 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists' (antecedent causes consequent); (2) 'If daytime exists, then the sun is rising' (consequent causes antecedent); (3) 'If daytime exists, then light is spread on the earth' (both are effects of a common cause).
Recognise that causality can flow in either direction or be parallel.
4
Understand correlation (taḍāyuf)
Examine the father-son example: 'If Zayd is the father of 'Amr, then 'Amr is his son.' Here, each term is defined in relation to the other; the implication is based on mutual dependence.
The text mentions that this is called circular definition (al-dawr al-mu'ayy).
5
Distinguish causality from correlation
Contrast the examples: in causality, one term is the cause or effect; in correlation, the terms are interdependent without one causing the other.
In correlation, the truth of the antecedent and consequent is simultaneous and reciprocal.

The conditional proposition consists of two parts: the first is called the antecedent and the second is called the consequent. The reality of the connected conditional: It is the proposition that necessitates the concomitance and accompaniment between the two parts [the antecedent and consequent] in existence, either with or without a relationship, even if it is accidental. (1) You can conceive the accidental from its previous example: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying.' You notice that there is no relation between man being rational and the donkey braying, so there is no concomitance between them. However, this connected conditional is correct because its correctness does not require a relation between the necessary and the necessary; it suffices merely the assumption of the truth of the consequent on the condition of the truth of the antecedent due to pure coincidence that its two parts occurred together, as here. This is unlike if you say: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists,' for there is a clear relation between the necessary and the necessary. So the accidental (ittiāqiyya) is the proposition in which the truth of the consequent is judged on the condition of the truth of the antecedent, without a relation that necessitates that, but merely due to their truth. Accordingly, the connected conditional is divided into accidental, which you have known, and implicative (luzūmiyya), which is the one in which the truth of the consequent is judged on the condition of the truth of the antecedent due to a relation that necessitates that, i.e., the reason by which the antecedent implies the consequent, such as causality and correlation. The Three Types of Disjunctive Conditionals
Defines the disjunctive conditional and its three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunctive conditionals (māni'at jam', māni'at khuluw, māni'at jam' wa khuluw) and identify their logical composition.

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditionals differ by whether they prevent joint truth, joint falsehood, or both.

In this lesson, we explore the disjunctive conditional, a proposition that asserts mutual exclusion between two parts. The author defines it and then breaks it down into three distinct types, each with its own logical structure and example. Understanding these types is crucial for analysing disjunctive arguments in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of a disjunctive conditional
The passage states it requires 'mutual exclusion and opposition between the two parts in truth or falsehood or both.' This is the core property.
2
Analyse the first type: māni'at jam'
This type prevents the two parts from being true together. It is composed of 'the thing and the more specific than its contradictory.' Example: 'This is either black or white.' Note that both can be false (e.g., red).
The key is that it only prohibits simultaneous truth, not simultaneous falsehood.
3
Analyse the second type: māni'at khuluw
This type prevents both parts from being false together; at least one must be true. It is composed of 'the thing and the more general than its contradictory.' Example: 'This is either not black or not white.' Both can be true (e.g., red is not black and not white), but they cannot both be false.
Contrast: this one prohibits simultaneous falsehood, not truth.
4
Analyse the third type: māni'at jam' wa khuluw
This type prevents both simultaneous truth and simultaneous falsehood; the two parts are exact contradictories. Composed of 'the thing and its contradictory.' Example: 'This number is either even or odd.' It is the true representative of contradiction.
This is the most restrictive type, combining the properties of the first two.
5
Compare the three types
The third type is 'more specific' than the first two, as it has both restrictions. Note the composition: first uses 'more specific than contradictory', second uses 'more general than contradictory', third uses the exact contradictory.
This comparison helps in identifying which type a given disjunctive belongs to.

Source Text

الثالث: مانعة جمع وخلو، وهي قضية تمنع الجمع والخلو معاً فلا يمكن اجتماع طرفيها ولا يمكن ارتفاعها، وتتركب من الشيء ونقيضه كقولنا هذا العدد إما زوج أو فرد، فلا يمكن اجتماع الزوجية والفردية في العدد المعين ولا يمكن ارتفاعها عنه.
وهذه القضية هي الممثلة الحقيقية للتعاند، لأن التعاند متحقق بين الطرفين في الصدق والكذب بخلاف ما قبله فإنه في أحدهما فقط، وهذه القضية أخص من كلا النوعين اللذين قبلها (1).
